Great Tower Street, E.C. 1906. LON 37 REPORT OF THE MEDICAL OFFICER OF HEALTH FOR THE CITY OF LONDON For the Year 1905, BY Wm. COLLINGRIDGE, M.A., M.D., D.P.H., LL.M., medical officer of health for the city of london, late medical officer of health for the port of london. LONDON: Printed by Charles Skipper & East, 49, Great Tower Street, E.C. 1906. VAUGHAN MORGAN, Mayor. A Common Council holden in the Chamber of the Guildhall of the City of London, on Thursday, the 29th day of March, 1906. THE Sanitary Committee did this day deliver into this Court a Report in writing under their hands, submitting the Annual Report of the Medical Officer of Health for the City of London for the year 1905; and recommending that the same be printed and circulated for the information of the Court as usual, and a copy thereof forwarded to the Local Government Board and the London County Council. Agreed to, and Ordered accordingly. BELL. CONTENTS. (Meat Shed) ... ... ... ... •• ••• ••• May, 1905 REPORT OF MEDICAL OFFICER OF HEALTH i FOR THE CITY OF LONDON, 1905. ANNUAL REPORT OF THE MEDICAL OFFICER OF HEALTH FOR THE YEAR 1905, To the Right Honourable the Lord Mayor, Aldermen and Commons of the Corporation of London. Public Health Department, Guildhall, E.C., March, 1906. Gentlemen, In conformity with the requirements of the Local Government Board, I have the honour to present my sixth Annual Report as Medical Officer of Health for the City of London. Some of the more important matters dealt with by my Department in the year under review are briefly referred to in the following introductory observations. Much attention has been given to the character of the milk supply to the City, which, unfortunately, has again been shown to leave much room for improvement in the manner of its control and distribution. On p. 31 will be found the result of a further series of bacteriological analyses of milk samples from 22 different Counties from which milk is sent to the City. These again show unmistakeably the great need for a stricter supervision of dairy farms. With regard to contamination of shellfish by sewage, a case of much importance to the public has quite recently been the subject of litigation. In this suit the lessee of some shellfish layings was successful in obtaining damages from a seaside local authority, together with an injunction restraining them from polluting layings with sewage. This decision will, it is stated, be the subject of appeal to a higher Court, and as the details will, no doubt, be fully discussed in the public Press, the matter is not further referred to in this Report. An active administration of the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts has been continued, and some tables will be found herein showing how the City compares with other Metropolitan districts with regard to the amount of adulteration practised in each locality. Some account is also given of the methods of manufacture of sophisticated butter, which supporters of a Pure Butter Bill would do well to give attention to. The question of warranties has been much in evidence during the year, and unscrupulous traders find them a ready means of evading the consequences of their acts. In no less than nine instances last year in the City the production of a warranty by defendants led to the cases against them being dismissed. Similar defences have been successful elsewhere, and it is clear that unless some amendment or repeal of the existing warranty clauses of the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts are made, adulteration, especially of milk, will flourish exceedingly. In the course of the year the Corporation was asked to subscribe to a recommendation to transfer the administration of the Vaccination Acts and 10 the Registration of Births and Deaths from the Boards of Guardians in London to the Borough Councils. The Sanitary Committee approved the suggestion, which was made by the Finsbury Borough Council, for reasons stated in this Report. The final test of the furnace at the Crematorium erected by the Corporation at the City of London Cemetery, Little Ilford, was carried out on the 3rd March, in the presence of a medical representative of the Home Office. The first human cremation took place on the 18th March, and in the course of the year there were eight others. From careful weighings it has been ascertained that the average percentage of ash was 2.86 of the weight of the body, the average time for the completion of incineration being 63 minutes. On p. 54 will be found a chart showing the development of this method of disposing of the dead since cremation received legal sanction in 1884. The opinions of several persons in charge of Crematoria in different parts of the Kingdom as to the probability of cremation becoming more general are also given, from which it will be seen, as might have been expected, that the more intelligent class of the community are less prejudiced against an ideal method of destroying dead matter without danger to the living than others less favoured. It will be noted that the meat seizures during the year have been exceedingly heavy, and have consequently thrown a severe strain upon the resources of the staff engaged in meat inspection. Much of the Report is taken up with details of the working of the sanitary provisions of the Factory and Workshop Act by your Inspectors. It will be seen on p. 25 that in view of the general increase of organised effort to reduce infantile mortality, one of your Women Inspectors is engaged in visiting all houses in the City where births have occurred, in order to instruct mothers as to the best methods of rearing children, especially during the first twelve months of life. AREA. The City of London Registration District comprises an area of 672.7 acres (excluding tidal water and foreshore), or 1.051 square miles, equal to a square of 1.0252 miles to a side. The area of "Registration London" is 74,817 acres (excluding tidal water and foreshore), or 116.9 square miles, equal to a square of 10.81 miles to a side. DENSITY (1905). City of London, Registration District, at night 33.3 persons to an acre. „ „ „ „ during the day *550 „ „ Registration London, at night 62.6 „ „ „ „ during the day not known. No Imperial Day Census has yet been taken. * This figure refers only to persons "actively engaged in the City during the daytime." The actual number of people visiting the City daily is probably nearly 1,500,000. 11 ANNUAL RATEABLE VALUE. The assessed rateable value of property within the administrative county of London was £41,657,244 on 6th April, 1905—whilst on the same date the corresponding rateable value of property in the City of London, including Temples, was £5,107,182, or nearly one-eighth of the whole County. This consists of two sub-districts, together containing a population of at the middle of 1905, calculated on the last Census Returns:— *St. Botolph 7,853 †St. Sepulchre and Allhallows 14,572 Total 22,425 The Night population, therefore, of the City of London Registration District at the middle of 1905 is taken at 22,425, and it is upon this figure that all vital statistics in this Report have been calculated. The Day population at the middle of 1905 was estimated at 369,380 ‡ persons. A Day Census taken in May, 1891, proved that 1,186,000 persons and 92,000 vehicles entered and left the City on the day the counting was made. In 1903 it was ascertained that in the week ending 19th July no less than 347,463 vehicles passed to and from the four City bridges, viz.:—London, Southwark, Blackfriars and Tower, equivalent to upwards of 18,000,000 vehicles per annum. BIRTHS. During the year 1905, 302 births were registered in the City. The annual birth rate was at the rate of 13.4 per 1,000 persons of all ages, compared with a quinquennial mean rate of 13.9 per 1,000. The birth rates last year in the Sub-Districts were— St. Botolph 17.8 per 1,000. St. Sepulchre and Allhallows 11.5 „ In the Metropolis the birth rate in 1905 was 27.1 per 1,000, being the lowest hitherto recorded therein. This decline in the birth rate has been in operation since 1871 or 1872, and is common to nearly all European countries. * At the date of the last Census, this District included the "civil parish" of Glasshouse Yard. The parish was transferred to the Holborn Union by the Local Government Board Order, No. 43156, which came into operation on 29th September, 1901, and to the Registration District of Holborn (Finsbury Sub-District) on 1st January, 1902, making the Registration District of London City co-extensive with the Municipal City of London. All the Kegistration Districts in London are co-extensive with the Poor-Law Parishes or Unions except the District of Fulham. † On the 1st April, 1901, the Middle Temple was transferred from the Strand Sub-District to St. Sepulchre Sub-District. ‡ This figure is based upon the assumption that the number of persons residing, occupied or employed within the City during the active hours of the day has increased at the same rate since the last Day Census in 1891 that it did between that and the previous Day Census in 1881. 13 VACCINATION. Of the children born in 1905, 192 had been successfully vaccinated by the end of the year, and 27 had died before the age for vaccination. Vaccination was postponed by medical certificate in 11 cases, 32 had removed into other districts, and 36 vaccinations were pending at the end of the year. Nil. 8.9 3.6 22.5 1.3 MORTALITY. During the year under review, 896 deaths were registered in the District, including *675 persons dying in the City, but not belonging thereto. See Table, p. 17, for details. Deducting the latter we have to deal with 221 deaths, to which we must add 92 persons who died at City Institutions in outside districts, where they were registered. The mortality, therefore, upon which our death rate is calculated is 313. The death rate for 1905 was 13.9 per 1,000 per annum of the population (the previous year being 12.9), whilst that for the whole of the Metropolis was 15.1. These figures are not corrected for sex and age distribution. Death Rates, per 1,000 per Annum. Year. City of London. Great Towns of England and Wales. The deaths from zymotic disease per 1,000 of the population was 0.44 compared with 1.70 in the Metropolis. Infantile Mortality. The deaths of children under one year of age to 1,000 births were 139, against 129 for the whole of London. The Table of infantile mortality on p. 15 shows the deaths of children under one year arranged according to monthly age periods. From this it will be seen that no less than 31 per cent. of these deaths occurred in the first month, 38 per cent. within the first three months, and 69 per cent. in the first six months of existence. Deaths in City Institutions. During 1905, 92 persons died in City Workhouses, Workhouse Infirmaries, or Lunatic Asylums, or no less than 29.4 per cent. of the whole City mortality, the corresponding figure for the Metropolis (1904) being 20.8 per cent. This high percentage is caused in the City by the excessive proportion the number of inmates in these places (exclusive of officers and their families) bears to our population, the ratio being about 1 : 20, as compared with 1 : 94 in the Metropolis (Census 1901). In other words, these figures represent that one in every 20 citizens is an inmate of either a Workhouse, Workhouse Infirmary or a Lunatic Asylum, in comparison with one in every 94 inhabitants of the Metropolis. In 32 instances, or about 40 per cent., insanitary conditions of more or less importance were reported and duly remedied. SHELTER FOR TEMPORARY ACCOMMODATION DURING DISINFECTION. The Shelter in Golden Lane, Barbican, provided in accordance with Sec. 60 (4) of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, which enacts that "sanitary "authorities shall provide free of charge temporary shelter or house "accommodation with necessary attendants for the members of any family "in which any dangerous infectious disease has appeared, for the purpose of "enabling such dwellings to be disinfected by the sanitary authority," was injured by the fire which occurred at some premises in rear on 29th October, 1904. Although the necessary repairs are not yet complete, three families have been received. They numbered eight persons, of whom six were adults and two were children, and were housed during the disinfection of their homes after— Scarlatina Two families. Phthisis One family. One family came from a home consisting of three rooms, and two families from homes of two rooms. REGISTRATION OF BIRTHS AND DEATHS AND VACCINATION. SUGGESTED TRANSFER OF DUTIES. On 23rd January, 1905, a letter was received from the Finsbury Borough Council, stating that they approved the principle of a suggestion made by the Deptford Borough Council that the duties at present performed by Board of Guardians in London, in connection with the registration of births and deaths, should be transferred to Borough Councils and the registration effected at the Town Hall or municipal offices instead of at the private residence of the Registrars. They also stated that they agreed with the principle of a further suggestion made by the same Borough Council, that the administration of the Vaccination Acts should also be similarly transferred. The Finsbury Borough Council hoped both proposals would meet with the approval of the Corporation. The matter having been referred to me for consideration, I reported to the Sanitary Committee that I was entirely in accord with both propositions. With regard to the first, the whole question of registration is so intimately associated with the satisfactory compilation of vital statistics that it is evident the work should be carried out by those concerned in the administration of the Public Health Acts. Under the existing system, or 22 rather lack of system, considerable delay and expense is incurred by Sanitary Authorities in obtaining information from registrars of subdistricts that is indispensable for the proper discharge of their duties. A further disadvantage is that at present the registrars are only available to the public during very limited hours. There is a lack of dignity attaching to the present mode of conducting the statutory obligations of registration at private houses and retailers' shops, which is incompatible with a modern sense of the fitness of things, and the local Town Hall, Guildhall, or other public building is the proper place for the purpose. As to the manner in which the various Acts relating to vaccination are carried out, I made no criticism of the manner in which they were administered by the Guardians, except to point out that the last returns then issued by the Local Government Board showed that in London the number of cases not finally accounted for as regards vaccination amounted to no less than 25.8 per cent. of the number of births. I advocated this transfer in a Report in which the Sanitary Committee concurred in January, 1902. It is too lengthy for reproduction here, but time has only strengthened the views then set forth. The financial question involved, if the suggested administrative duties are transferred to the Borough Councils, hardly comes within my province, but I do not anticipate there would be any difficulty in so apportioning the rates that the cost incurred in each case would be paid by the Borough Councils and a corresponding reduction made in the Poor Rate levy. INFANTILE MORTALITY. On the 5th June, 1905, a Conference of Imperial, County and Municipal representatives of St. Pancras, Medical Men and Nurses, Ministers of Religion and District Visitors, Philanthropists and Social Workers, was held at the Town Hall, Pancras Road, N.W., at the initiation of the St. Pancras Borough Council, to consider the question of Infantile Mortality. The following Resolutions were considered and agreed to:— A.—Mothers. 1.—That it is desirable that every encouragement should be given to prospective mothers to seek advice as to the management of their health so that they may endow their offspring with good constitutions and prepare themselves to suckle their infants when born. 2.—That it is desirable that all efforts should be exhausted upon improving the health of suckling mothers before devoting attention solely to their infants. 23 B.—Breast-Feeding of Infants. 1.—That it is desirable that births should be registered within five days of their occurrence as is required in the case of deaths by the Births and Deaths Registration Acts, and be not delayed for six weeks and longer as now by law permitted. 2.—That it is desirable that the municipal authority should obtain weekly returns of births from the registrars. 3.—That it is desirable that the municipal authority should send to the addresses of all mothers, furnished in the weekly returns, cards or leaflets of advice, laying greatest stress on the improvement of antinatal conditions, on the preparation for and maintenance of breastfeeding of infants during the first nine months of life, and strongly advising mothers not to wean their infants prematurely except upon medical advice. 4.—That it is desirable that women inspectors should be appointed with a view to the prevention of infantile mortality by selecting from the weekly returns suitable cases amongst the poor for inquiry, affording general information in the hygiene of nursing, and directing the necessitous poor where to apply for such advice and assistance as may be urgently required in order to maintain breast-feeding. 5.—That it is desirable that medical practitioners, midwives, and nurses, the staffs of hospitals and dispensaries, and the religious, charitable and social organisations should co-operate with the municipal authority in endeavouring to encourage breast-feeding, and to diminish the infantile mortality. 6.—That it is desirable that any suggestions or arrangements made by medical practitioners or by the medical charities for the purpose of advising nursing mothers with a view to preventing infantile mortality should be communicated to the municipal authority in order that they may be made known through the women inspectors. 7.—That it is desirable that the information should be spread broadcast that breast-feeding is the most economical, easy, and safe mode of feeding infants under nine months of age, with a view to reducing the number of hand-fed sucklings to the smallest proportion possible. C.—Premature Weaning. 1.—That it is undesirable that a suckling mother should be separated from her infant, or the infant from the mother, except for reasons of ill-health and under medical advice. 2.—That it is desirable that when a woman inspector has reason to believe that a suckling mother is in a necessitous condition and unprovided with sufficient food to enable her to maintain her own strength and suckle her infant as well, she should endeavour to prevent weaning by reporting the case to the charitable organisation most appropriate to deal with the circumstances. 24 3.— That it is desirable that when a woman inspector finds that a suckling mother has insufficient nourishment due to the husband being out of work, she should report the case to the labour bureau. 4.— That it is desirable that mothers should be urged not to wean their infants until the ninth month of age except upon medical advice. 5.— That it is desirable that mothers should be warned that the hand-feeding of infants is expensive, troublesome, and unsatisfactory, risky at all times, and in summer dangerous and often fatal. 6.— That with a view not to encourage premature weaning, it is desirable that milk for the hand-feeding of infants provided privately or publicly by milk depots at or below cost should be distributed upon the orders of medical practitioners. D.— Hand-Feeding of Infants. 1.— That, having reduced the number of sucklings required to be hand-fed to the smallest proportion possible, means should be devised for providing suitable milk for the remainder. 2.— That it is undesirable to encourage the use of pasteurised or sterilised milk for the use of hand-fed infants who unfortunately cannot be fed from the breast. 3.— That it is desirable that cow's milk intended for hand-fed infants should be milked, transported, and distributed under special precautions. 4.— That the special precautions required in the production of cow's milk for infants should include not only care in the selection, testing, and feeding of the cows, but also care in their pasturing, sheltering and tending, not only extreme cleanliness in milking, but also rigid aseptic precautions, accompanied by immediate refrigeration, maintained during transport, and by prompt dispatch so that the number of microorganisms and the lactic acid in the milk may be reduced to a minimum at the time of delivery instead of the hundreds of thousands and millions of organisms and excess of lactic acid now to be found in so-called fresh milk. 5.— That, in order to prevent the pollution of infants' milk in distribution and in the home, it is desirable that it should be protected in stoppered Dottles ready for feeding, and not be transferred to other bottles. 6.— That on account of the facilities that hand-feeding affords for the overfeeding and under-feeding of infants, due to want of knowledge, especially amongst the poor, it is desirable that the quantity and quality of milk required for each feed at the particular age should be distributed in a separate stoppered bottle, and that each basket or crate of bottles for use should be accompanied by written instructions as to the frequency and manner of feeding, and that such instructions should be supplemented by oral instructions given by women inspectors or health visitors at the houses of the milk consumers. 25 7.— That the provision of cow's milk produced under aseptic conditions accompanied by extreme cleanliness, refrigeration and dispatch, so diluted and enriched and put up in stoppered bottles in such quantities to be administered at specified intervals suitable for suckling infants, should be regarded not only as ordinary food, but in the nature of medical assistance. 8.— That for the special protection of hand-fed infants it is desirable that Municipal Authorities should not only ascertain the quality of milk intended for infants by chemical analysis, and receive from the public analyst a statement of the percentage composition of each sample of milk submitted, as to fat, solids not fat, and total solids, and the absence of chemical preservatives, but also by bacteriological examination, and receive from a bacteriologist a statement in regard to each sample of milk submitted as to the number of micro-organisms per cubic centimetre and the proportion of lactic acid per cent., these being the best existing tests of cleanliness and freshness of the milk. With the whole of the above Resolutions, anyone with a knowledge of the importance of the subject must be in complete accord, and the only points for consideration are those of detail in carrying out the scheme. It should, however, be pointed out that underlying the whole question is the danger, from a public health point of view, of introducing in a subtle manner the evil of municipal trading. As I have previously pointed out when reporting on the Milk Depots (London) Bill in March, 1905, that in view of the opinion of the Committee on Physical Deterioration that inasmuch as any difficulty in obtaining pure and sterilised milk can be got over without recourse to direct municipal action, any such trading by public authorities would be both an unnecessary and costly charge upon the rates. Moreover, the proper function of local authorities is to supervise traders rather than to enter into what must necessarily be an unfair business competition with them. Owing to the small number of inhabitants, and therefore children in the City, the matter is not one of imminent importance so far as the City inhabitants are concerned; but in view of the general increase of organised effort now made to save these young lives, which are now doubly valuable to the State owing to the continued decrease in the birth rate, Miss Pole, one of your Women Sanitary Inspectors, who has previous experience of this work, has undertaken the duty of visiting all houses where births have been notified by the Registrars, and also those where deaths of children under one year of age have occurred. The main object of these visits is to impress upon mothers the great advantage of breast-feeding as compared with hand-feeding. It has been shown that in some large towns the mortality during the first year of life among breast-fed infants was only about one-third of that among hand-fed children. It is impossible to overrate the significance of these figures, which clearly indicate a great loss of life from a preventable cause. 26 While improved sanitation has reduced the death rate in London from 21.2 per 1,000 in 1881 to 16.6 in 1904, the deaths of children under one year of age per 1,000 births, which were 148 in the former year, reached almost the same high proportion, viz., 146, in 1904. It is now well recognised that this heavy infantile mortality is due to ignorance on the part of the mothers as to the danger incurred by injudicious feeding, and the efforts now being made in the direction of educating women, especially those of the poorer classes, in the proper manner of rearing their offspring during the first twelve months of their existence will, it is hoped, remove what is now undoubtedly a blot upon our civilization. A Table is given on page 15 showing the deaths of children in the City during 1905 under one year, arranged according to monthly age periods. PREVENTION OF TUBERCULOSIS. INTERNATIONAL CONGRESS IN PARIS. In July last, at the invitation of the National Association for the Prevention of Consumption and other forms of Tuberculosis, your Medical Officer was appointed as delegate to represent the Corporation at the International Congress on Tuberculosis which was held in Paris, October 2nd— 7th, under the patronage of the President of the French Republic. The meetings of the Congress were held within the above-mentioned dates, at the Grand Palais, Avenue d'Antin, Champs Elysées, under the Presidency of Dr. Herard. The work of the Congress was divided into four sections, of which sections 1, 2 and 3 respectively dealt with the Medical Pathology of Tuberculosis, the Surgical Pathology of Tuberculosis, and the Prevention and Treatment of Tuberculosis in Children, while section 4 was separated into two subsections, of which one considered the Prevention and Treatment of Tuberculosis in Adults, and the other was devoted to Social Hygiene. The fourth section was the one possessing most attractions for those interested in public health questions. The following resolutions were passed by this section, after a discussion on the part played by sanatoriums and dispensaries in the struggle against Tuberculosis:— 1. It is permissible to discuss to what degree dispensaries and sanatoriums are useful or necessary according to the customs, the institutions, and resources of each country, but the principle involved must be recognised. 2. They constitute a means of action which cannot be considered either as exclusive or predominant; dispensaries open to all have for their essential objective prophylaxy, hygienic education, and the relief of distress. They can also become a valuable source of information. The popular sanatoriums are establishments 27 reserved for in-patients suffering from pulmonary Tuberculosis, who are susceptible of permanent cure or a durable amelioration; they are likewise instruments of prophylaxy and education. But the problem of sanitary dwellings will always dominate the prophylaxy of Tuberculosis. 3. It is important that the functions of these establishments should, as far as possible, and while not infringing their freedom and their anatomy, be made to work in harmony with a general scheme. They can only benefit by being connected one with another and by being placed in relation with allied institutions such as public health and benefit societies. 4. As desiderata to be realised at the same time as the development of dispensaries and sanatoria, the Congress would urge the necessity of certain reforms: (a) That poor relief should assume a more preventive character and take a more active part in promoting good sanitation. (b) That friendly societies should also endeavour to exercise a direct action in bringing about a more rational application of precautionary and sanitary measures. With regard to Tuberculosis in relation to the housing question, the sections subscribed to the following recommendations:— 1. Municipalities should adopt the necessary measures to encourage the use in the dwellings of the poor of an abundant and cheap water supply. 2. To combat the tendency to the production of Tuberculosis by overcrowding it is desirable to augment the number of workmen's gardens and of public gardens, to increase the open spaces, to plant trees in open spaces of the large towns, and to resist all that tends to increase the crowding into towns while encouraging a return to the open fields. The following propositions were adopted with reference to Tuberculosis and disinfection of dwellings:— 1. It is desirable that it should be the general practice to notify all cases of advanced Tuberculosis. 2. The means of disinfection taken in cases of advanced Tuberculosis should be in keeping with the methods and with the aid of the processes that have been found to be most effective. 3. The administrative ordinances, or, if necessary, the legislative enactments which shall sanction and ensure the practice of disinfection, should be promulgated with the least possible delay. PROPOSED SANATORIA FOR CONSUMPTIVES. In May, 1905, the Metropolitan Asylums Board forwarded for the consideration of the Corporation a copy of a memorial which the Managers had received from the Metropolitan Branch of the Incorporated Society of Medical Officers of Health on the subject of the provision by the Metropolitan Asylums Board of hospital accommodation for the treatment and isolation of patients suffering from pulmonary Phthisis, and asked to be furnished with any observations that the Corporation might desire to offer. 28 This communication was referred to your Medical Officer of Health, who reported as follows to the Sanitary Committee on the 25th July, 1905:— "PROPOSED SANATORIA FOR CONSUMPTIVES. "This is a matter of the highest importance from a public health point of view, "and one that has been under consideration for some considerable period. Involv"ing, as it must of necessity do, the expenditure of a very large sum of money, and "that obtainable only from the ratepayer, it is essential that the most careful "consideration be given to the scheme. It is scarcely needful to refer to the "necessity for some such provision. The fact alone that the deaths from Phthisis in "London amount to between 7,000 and 8,000 every year is sufficient to show the "vital importance of the question. Seven thousand deaths per annum would mean "approximately that 50,000 people are actually suffering from the disease in "London at the same time, and for these the only provision are four Consumption "Hospitals. "Hospital provision is required for two classes of cases. Firstly, those in the "early stages of the disease, which should be isolated for the purpose of bringing "them under hygienic conditions and treatment with a view to the cure or arrest of "the disease; and, secondly, those in a more advanced stage, where the risk of "infection is most marked, and which should be isolated so far as possible to "prevent danger to their fellow-creatures. "Both classes require consideration. It is not suggested (and it certainly is not "advisable), that costly and palatial hospitals be provided for these cases, but rather "that inexpensive buildings situated in healthy neighbourhoods at some little "distance from the Metropolis be erected for their accommodation. The use of "such sanatoria as suggested is twofold. In the first place the patient is removed "from conditions in which he may be a serious danger to others; and, secondly, "during the stay in such institution he is accustomed to hospital discipline, and is "instructed as to the best methods of preserving his own health and minimising "the possible danger to others. In other words, the object of the treatment is both "remedial and preventive. "It has been estimated that every bed provided will accommodate two persons "per annum, and that of the 50,000 who are suffering from Tuberculosis at any "given time, there are probably 10,000 to whom remedial treatment is of value, "therefore 5,000 beds would be sufficient to deal with these cases. The maintenance "alone of these would cost— at an estimated expenditure of 25s. per week per head— "£325,000 per annum. There is also the cost of provision of the buildings, which "would probably be covered by £100 for each bed— i.e., a capital expenditure of at "least £500,000. As to the question of the authority by whom this work "should be undertaken, only two such have been suggested, viz., the Metro"politan Asylums Board and the London County Council, and there can, "I think, be no difference of opinion as to the suitability of the Metropolitan "Asylums Board rather than the London County Council being entrusted with this "if it be undertaken. In the first place because the London County Council, "already overburdened with work, have no power under the Isolation Hospitals "Act, 1893, which does not apply to London, and, moreover, is not a sanitary "authority. The Metropolitan Asylums Board have, further, had great experience "in dealing with epidemic diseases, and have carried out this large and responsible "work for a long period to the satisfaction of the ratepayers of the Metropolis 29 "Probably the simplest plan would be to obtain an order from the Local Govern"ment Board under Section 5 of the Metropolitan Poor Act, 1867:— "'Asylums to be supported and managed according to the provisions of this "'Act may be provided under this Act for Reception and Relief of the Sick, "'Insane, or Infirm, or other class or classes of the Poor, chargeable in Unions "'and Parishes in the Metropolis (and in this Act the term "Asylum" means "'an Asylum provided under this Act),' "to enable them to deal with suitable cases of the chargeable poor suffering from "Phthisis, and to obtain an amendment of section 80 of the Public Health Act, 1891, "to enable them to deal with non-chargeable cases. "This section reads as follows:— "'The Metropolitan Asylums Managers, subject to such regulations and "'restrictions as the Local Government Board prescribe, may admit any "'person, who is not a pauper, and is reasonably believed to be suffering from " 'Fever or Small-pox or Diphtheria into the hospitals provided by the "'Managers.' "The addition of the word "or tuberculous Phthisis" after Diphtheria would effect "this. "I may mention that the advisability of the Metropolitan Asylums Board being "made the authority for dealing with Phthisis in the case of persons chargeable to "the poor rates was agreed to by a conference, to which (among others) the Metro"politan Medical Officers of Health and the Medical Superintendents of the Poor "Law Infirmaries were invited, held on the 9th January, 1905, when the following "Resolutions were passed:— "'(1) That it is of urgent importance that further and more complete public "'provision be made for cases of Phthisis. "'(2) That it is desirable that provision be made by the Metropolitan "'Asylums Board for persons suffering from Phthisis who are or may from " 'time to time become chargeable to the poor rates. "'(3) That the Council of the Charity Organisation Society approach the "'following Societies, amongst others, with a view to these or similar resolu"'tions being submitted to the Local Government Board, jointly, viz., the "'Society of Medical Officers of Health, the National Association for the "'Prevention of Consumption, the National Health Society, the Royal Sanitary "'Institute.' "The Society of Medical Officers of Health organised a deputation on these lines. "Resolutions in favour of the Metropolitan Asylums Board being the authority "were also passed by the Society of Medical Officers of Health in October, 1903— "by the delegates of the Metropolitan Borough Councils at a Conference convened "by the London County Council held in July, 1904— and by the National Society "for the Prevention of Consumption, in March, 1905. "At a conference held in October, 1900, at which 20 of the 32 Metropolitan "Boards of Guardians were represented, the following Resolutions were passed:— "'That this conference of Poor Law Authorities, having discussed the "'question of the open-air treatment of Consumption, are of opinion that the 30 "'time has come when provision should be made for the treatment by this "'method of the Sick Poor of the Metropolis suffering from Phthisis, and "'it recommends the matter for the consideration of the Local Government "'Board with a view to the necessary steps being taken by the Board for the "'carrying out of the proposal.' "The matter then resolves itself into a question of public health, on which "the Corporation is now asked to give an opinion. From the public health point of "view it seems absolutely essential that some reasonable precautions should be "taken, and that the Metropolitan Asylums Board should be called upon to make "the same." The Corporation having concurred in these views, the Metropolitan Asylums Board were in due course informed "that the Corporation is of "opinion that the provision of a Sanatorium for Consumptives is absolutely "essential as a reasonable precaution to public health, and that the Metro"politan Asylums Board is the proper authority for dealing with it." At a Meeting held on the 17th February, 1906, the General Purposes Committee of the Metropolitan Asylums Board submitted a report stating that with the object of ascertaining in the first instance the views of the Local Government Board, administrative bodies of Borough Councils, Boards of Guardians, and Hospitals for Consumption upon the subject, the Committee gave directions that the Boards of Guardians should also be asked what steps, if any, they had taken to deal with tuberculous cases in their infirmaries, and also with what result. The Local Government Board replied, under date 27th June, 1905, to the effect that they were then "obtaining information on the whole "subject." They were not "in a position to express an opinion on the "question as to whether the Managers should undertake the new duties "suggested." Of the 65 other authorities consulted, 19 were wholly favourable to the prayer of the memorial, 9 were favourable if the cost were made a national charge, 14 were unfavourable, and the replies of the remaining 23 were indefinite. In the absence of any authoritative expression of opinion by the Local Government Board, and as a result of its consideration of the question in all its bearings, the Committee recommended, and the Board of Managers adopted the following:— "(1) That the Managers do forward to the Local Government Board a copy of "the memorials addressed to the Managers (a) by the Metropolitan Branch of the "Incorporated Society of Medical Officers of Health on the 15th April, 1905; and "(b) by Sir William Broadbent, Bart., K.C.V.O., M.D., F.R.S., on the 4th "November last on the subject of the provision of hospital accommodation for the "treatment and isolation of patients suffering from Pulmonary Phthisis. "(2) That the Local Government Board be further informed that, having regard "to the uncertainty which prevails as to the probable number of patients who would "come under the schemes referred to in the memorials, and to the great prospective "cost to the ratepayers of the metropolis, the Managers will be glad to receive the "judgment of the Local Government Board upon the prayer of the memorials "referred to." 31 CONTAMINATED MILK SUPPLIES. In my Annual Report for last year the results were given of a bacteriological examination of a series of samples of milk representing the supply to the City from the following 22 Counties:— Bedfordshire. Gloucestershire. Nottinghamshire. Berkshire. Hertfordshire. Oxfordshire. Buckinghamshire. Huntingdon. Somersetshire. Cambridgeshire. Leicestershire. Staffordshire. Derbyshire. Lincolnshire. Suffolk. Dorsetshire. Middlesex. Warwickshire. Essex. Norfolk. Wiltshire. Worcestershire. These samples yielded the following results:— Character of Milk. Number of Samples. Percentage. Clean and pure 19 48.7 Bacillus tuberculosis 3 7.9 Staphylococcus pyogenes aureus 7 17.9 Bacillus enteritidis Gaertner 10 25.5 Total 39 samples. 100.0 This examination showed that much of the milk supplied to the City was seriously contaminated. It was therefore considered advisable to make a further investigation of the character of milk consigned to City dealers, and in August last another series of milk samples for the undermentioned 22 Counties was taken, viz.:— Berkshire. Huntingdon. Surrey. Buckinghamshire. Hampshire. Somersetshire. Cambridgeshire. Leicestershire. Staffordshire. Derbyshire. Norfolk. Warwickshire. Dorsetshire. Northamptonshire. Wiltshire. Essex. Oxfordshire. Worcestershire. Gloucestershire. Rutland. Hertfordshire. Suffolk. These samples were submitted to Dr. Klein, who reported as follows:— "The appended Tables give the summary of results obtained by "injecting guinea-pigs with the milk delivered here by your Inspector "May between August 2nd and August 23rd inclusive. As in the "former tests, of each sample 300 cubic centimetres were allowed to "sediment, and this sediment was injected in about equal proportions "into one guinea-pig peritoneally, and into a second guinea-pig " subcutaneously. "As will be noticed in the Tables, several animals died spontaneously, "viz.:— "Guinea-pig 5a.— Subcutaneously injected with sample 5. This "animal showed extensive pneumonia, but no microbes could be "discovered. 32 "Guinea-pig 8a.— Subcutaneously injected with sample 8. This "animal showed typical septicæmia with copious presence of "bacillus coli communis in the blood and in all viscera. "Guinea-pig 17.— Peritoneally injected with sample 17. It showed "typical necrotic-purulent deposits in the omentum, liver and "spleen, with the copious presence of bacilli pseudo-tuberculosis. Guinea-pig 22.— Peritoneally injected with sample 22. Died of "acute peritonitis on August 24th, with copious presence of "bacillus coli communis. "The companion of this animal, viz., guinea-pig 22a, subcutaneously "injected with sample 22, developed big tumour in groin, which "became necrotic, leading to extensive ulceration of the skin of the "groin and abdomen (healed by September 18th), a condition "known to occur after subcutaneous injection of some virulent "forms of bacillus coli communis. "Of the other animals killed between three weeks and four weeks, "only those of sample 16 (sixteen) and 17 (seventeen) showed the "lesions of true tuberculosis, with the copious presence of the bacilli "tuberculosis."
