The following is a summary of the results obtained:— Character of Milk. Number of Samples. Percentage. Clean and pure milk 15 68 Unclean milk 5 23 Tubercular 2 9 Total 22 100 The percentage composition of the two series referred to above may be classified as under:— 1904. 1905. Clean and pure 49 per cent. 68 per cent. Unclean 43 „ 23 „ Tubercular 8 „ 9 „ 34 Although in the last series there is anincrease in the proportion of "clean" milks, and a corresponding decrease in the percentage of those found "unclean," the recurrence of such a heavy ratio of milk from tuberculous animals must give rise to serious misgivings. The City milk supplies have been examined on three occasions for the bacilli of Tuberculosis. First in 1902, when 24 samples were examined with negative results in each instance; and again in 1904 and 1905 with the results as stated above. The number of samples examined will scarcely justify the assumption that the percentage of tuberculous milk sent from the country to the City has risen from nothing in 1902 to 9 per cent. in 1905, but it may be fairly assumed under existing conditions that at least 8 per cent. of the milk sold in the City is derived from animals affected with Tuberculosis.* However much this fact is to be regretted, it can scarcely create surprise when it is remembered that out of 500 cows examined, after slaughter, by the City Veterinary Inspector some evidence of Tuberculosis was found in no less than 234 = 46.8 per cent.† The great necessity of a purer milk supply has been largely engaging the attention of sanitarians of recent years, but until the Dairies and Cowsheds Regulations are properly enforced throughout the country it is to be feared little improvement can be expected. The report of the Inspectors who have been sent by the Local Government Board to inquire into the sanitary conditions of various districts throughout England and Wales show that in very many instances little or no attempt is made to carry out the Orders in Council, which unfortunately are not mandatory. The conveyance of milk in insanitary railway vans is a source of contamination, with regard to which many complaints have been made to the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries, who state in their last Annual Report that during the year they had enquired into nine specific complaints. In one instance the Inspector of the Board saw milk being loaded in the same van with fish, and in another it was found that milk vans on a certain route frequently contained or smelt of fish. One other point may be noted: Section 71 (1) of the Public Health Act, 1875, makes provision for the protection of the public from the sale of milk "likely to cause infectious disease," but unfortunately action can only be taken when the disease is a "dangerous infectious disease"— in other words, diseases notifiable by statute— and Tuberculosis is not one of these; were it otherwise, this section might be of considerable service. A case of much importance to farmers was decided in the Tower Bridge Police Court on 12th May, 1905. The defendant, a farmer under contract to supply pure new milk to a firm of wholesale milk distributors, was summoned * This has been confirmed by further experiment. † Vide Annual Report, Veterinary Inspector, to the Cattle Markets Committee, for the year 1901. 35 by the St. Pancras Borough Council under Section 47 of the Public Health (London) Act, 1891, for depositing for sale at Euston Station milk that was unwholesome and unfit for food. It having been proved that the milk in question contained fæcal matter, pus cells, and bacilli of Tuberculosis, the defendant was fined 25l, and 5l. 5s. costs. The case is notable as being the first of the kind with regard to milk, and should have a good effect in awakening farmers to a sense of their responsibilities and the importance of cleanliness in handling milk. An appeal to the pocket to the extent of over £30 is likely to protect the public better than a benevolent admonition in cases of this kind. One important point requires consideration, viz., the advisability in the interests of public health of publishing the name of the dairyman, in any case where full information is not given as to the source of any contaminated supply, and where immediate investigation is not made as to the sanitary condition of the farm and the health of the cows, and where steps are not taken to discontinue the supply until any insanitary conditions found are removed. This is necessary, inasmuch as the consumers are unaware of the conditions under which the milk is produced, and are therefore unable to protect themselves against an undoubted danger to health. It is evident that generally the milk trade is conducted under most unsatisfactory conditions, there being no guarantee whatever that milk as usually sold is free from infection, or even that it is reasonably clean, as out of 61 samples examined during the past two years no less than 22, or 36 per cent., were contaminated with filth. The only remedy is to insist upon the Dairies and Cowsheds Regulations being enforced by Local Authorities, by giving powers to County Councils to act in default, and further, to sanction Central interference where this is not sufficient. In the meantime, I recommend that further evidence be obtained by bacterial examination of samples on the same lines, especially of those supplied by the Dairy Companies referred to in previous Reports. There is no difficulty in securing a clean milk supply if cowkeepers carry out the simple recommendations of the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries which the Board desire to be made known to all interested in the subject. These are embodied in a leaflet issued in July last, in which the subject is dealt with in a practical and common-sense manner. Attention is chiefly directed to cleanliness in the dairy, cleanliness in the management and housing of cows, and cleanliness in milking. The sources of bacterial contamination so inimical to milk are also clearly indicated, and photographs of plate cultivations graphically illustrate the very material differences which exist between dirty and cleanly cows, pails, and byres. The importance of "grooming" cows, especially in winter time, when they pass most of their time under cover, is insisted upon, and simple directions are given for keeping the animals under proper sanitary conditions, dealing 36 with air space, ventilation, water and supply. Precautionary measures for preventing contamination during milking are also detailed, and the importance of cleanliness in dairy utensils and the best means of cleansing them pointed out. Some good advice is also given to the milk sellers, both wholesale and retail, and I strongly recommend all those engaged in the production of milk and its distribution to read the pamphlet and carry out the recommendations of the Board. It may serve a useful purpose to mention that copies may be obtained, free of charge and post free, from the Secretary of the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries, No. 4, Whitehall Place. LIABILITY OF PERSONS SELLING INFECTED MILK. Frost v. Aylesbury Dairy Company. This case is of so much importance, both to the public and milk distributors, that the following notes which appeared in Public Health for April, 1905, are here reproduced. "HIGH COURT OF JUSTICE. "COURT OF APPEAL, 24th February, 1905. "Before Henn Collins, M.R., Mathew, and Cozens-Hardy, L.J J. "Infected Milk— Sale of— Breach of Warranty— Damages. "When milk is sold by a dairy company for consumption as food there is an implied "warranty on the part of the vendors that it is reasonably fit for that purpose, "and they will be held liable for damages caused by reason of its being infected, "although such infection was latent and not discoverable by ordinary care "and skill. "This was an appeal by the defendants asking for a new trial or judgment in an "action tried before Grantham, J., and a special jury, and reported in Public Health, "xvii. p. 260, where the facts are set out. The action was brought by the plaintiff "to recover damages owing to the illness and death of his wife from typhoid fever, "caused, as he alleged, by the milk which was supplied to him by the defendants "being contaminated with the germs of typhoid fever. The jury found a verdict "for the plaintiff for £106, the expenses to which the plaintiff had been put in "consequence of the illness and death of his wife; and judgment was entered for "him accordingly. "Macmorran K.C., and W. Mackenzie appeared for the defendants, and "contended that under Section 14 (1) of the Sale of Goods Act, 1893, the "defendants did not impliedly warrant that the milk was free from infection, as no "care and skill on their part could have discovered whether it was infected or not. "They also contended that the verdict was against the weight of the evidence, and "that the Judge at the trial had misdirected the jury and introduced topics of "prejudice against the defendants. "Duke, K.C., and Holman Gregory, for the plaintiff, were not called upon, and "the Court dismissed the appeal. 37 "The Master of the Rolls said that the first point taken was that, admitting that "the milk was the cause of the typhoid fever, upon the facts of the case there was "no actionable wrong on the part of the defendants. The question was whether, in "the particular circumstances, the case had been brought within Section 14. "Sub-section 1, of the Sale of Goods Act, 1893. It was said that the buyer had "not made known to the seller the particular purpose for which the goods were "required so as to show that he relied on the seller's skill or judgment. They had "not evidence in any precise detail of the inception of the relationship between the "plaintiff and the defendants. They began with the fact that the plaintiff was "dealing with the defendants. The result was that milk was purchased by the plaintiff from the defendants. That involved a contract for the supply of an "article of food— namely, milk. The purpose for which the milk was supplied "was obviously for consumption as an article of food. It did not require any "evidence to prove the particular purpose for which the milk was supplied. The "jury must be taken to have found that. Where was the condition so as to show "that the buyer relied on the seller's skill or judgment fulfilled? If any seller "ever did inform the mind of a buyer of his special care as a seller, as an "inducement to the would-be buyer to deal with him, those defendants did. The "book which had been called the pass-book, which was supplied to the plaintiff, "was full of particulars of their skill and knowledge in the matter. It contained "paragraphs headed 'Milk in relation to tuberculosis and other diseases'— stating "the precautions taken to ensure that only pure milk free from all germs of "disease and free from adulteration was supplied. One paragraph related to "'medical inspection,' another to 'milk analysis,' another to 'veterinary "surgeon.' The buyer could not escape being permeated with the sense that he "was secured against the possibility of danger if he bought the defendants' milk. "Milk, therefore, for the supply of the plaintiff and his family was tendered by a seller "with all this knowledge and skill. It was then said that the buyer could not rely "upon the sellers' skill or judgment in a case where no skill or judgment could "have found out the defect. That was a contention that a person could not "become liable for an undiscoverable latent defect. The law upon the point was "the same now as it was at common law before the Sale of Goods Act, 1893. "That point had been dealt with and decided by the Court of Appeal in Randall v. "Newson (2 Q.B.D., 102), where it was held that on the sale of an article for a "specific purpose there was a warranty by the vendor that it was reasonably fit for "the purpose, and that there was no exception as to latent undiscoverable defects. "Then, with regard to the alleged misdirection, it was a well-established rule that "even if there was misdirection, still if that misdirection did not affect the result, "of the trial, the Court would not be justified in sending the case down for a new "trial. Mr. Macmorran complained, and he (the Master of the Rolls) must admit "with some justice, that the learned Judge made some observations about the name "of the defendant company, though that had nothing to do with the case; and he "thought that it was unfortunate that the learned Judge had made those "observations. In his (the Master of the Rolls') opinion there was not the shadow "of a foundation for any aspersion on the defendants in connection with their "bearing the name of the Aylesbury Dairy Company. But those observations of "the learned Judge had no effect upon the case. He did not think that "depreciating the use by the defendants of the name had any effect on the case, "The circumstances of the case showed plainly the purpose for which the milk was "sold. The circumstances spoke for themselves, and it would be a waste of time "to ask a jury a specific question upon that particular point. With great respect "and deference to the learned Judge, he thought that his direction to the jury was 38 "capable of improvement, but it did not affect the result of the trial. The "application must therefore be dismissed. "Lord Justice Mathew delivered judgment to the same effect, in the course "of which he said that there was no reason whatever for any reflection upon "the defendant company. They had imposed stringent conditions upon the "farmers from whom they obtained the milk, but it was obviously impossible, "where their milk was drawn from different parts of the country, to see that each "farmer complied in every respect with their conditions and requirements. He "(the Lord Justice) regretted that the use of the word 'Aylesbury' in the name "of the defendant company was reflected upon by the learned Judge. He desired "to say that he did not concur in those reflections, and in his opinion the defendant "company came well out of the inquiry. "Lord Justice Cozens-Hardy agreed." THE SPITTING EVIL. The principal Railway Companies, with one exception, recast their Byelaws during the year, and, it is satisfactory to note, availed themselves of the opportunity to add Bye-laws making spitting on platforms, booking offices, &c., or in railway carriages, a punitive offence. In each case the wording of the new Bye-law is identical, and has been duly allowed and approved by the Board of Trade. It is to the following effect:— "No. 22.— No person shall spit upon the floor or upon any part of any carriage "using or being on the railway, or upon the platform at any station of the Company, "or upon the floor, side or wall of any hall, office, waiting-room, refreshment-room, "public room, or public passage at any station of the Company. Any person "infringing or not observing this bye-law and regulation shall be liable to the penalty "prescribed by Bye-law No. 1, and in case of non-compliance with the request of any "servant or agent of the Company to desist, may, without prejudice to any such "penalty, be forthwith removed from the Company's premises by or under the "direction of any such servant or agent. The penalty referred to is a fine not exceeding forty shillings for a first offence, and not exceeding £5 for any subsequent offence. Hitherto Railway Companies have been powerless to do more than appeal to a public sense of decency by exhibiting cautionary notices against spitting in carriages, &c., but unhappily there still exists a section of the community to whom such appeal is useless. The Railway Companies are now armed with a ready means of dealing with such persons, who can be made to pay smartly for indulging in indiscriminate expectoration, and it is to be hoped that the public will render the Companies every assistance in the enforcement of this Bye-law. TELEPHONE MOUTHPIECES. Representations having been made from time to time as to the danger of infection from the use of telephone mouthpieces in Public Call Offices, and it especially being suggested that Tuberculosis and 39 Diphtheria might in this way be passed on from one user to another, it was decided to investigate the question bacteriologically. With the sanction of the National Telephone Company, a number of their Call Offices were selected, the mouthpieces removed, placed in sterilized bottles and submitted to Dr. Klein for bacteriological examination. These were "washed out with 2-3 cubic centimetres of sterile distilled water by means of "sterile brushes, and the whole of the washing was injected in about equal "parts into two guinea-pigs: one peritoneally, one subcutaneously. The "washings were very dirty, containing a lot of mineral matter (dust) and "some soot. The object being to discover whether there were present any "tubercle bacilli in the mouthpieces, the animals were allowed to live for four "weeks, by which time any tubercle present could be readily discovered. The "animals of all these injections (24 in all) appeared during these four weeks "in normal condition, and showed no pathological symptom, either locally "or otherwise," and subsequent post-mortem examination showed that the animals were all free from Tubercle and Diphtheria. In all, twelve mouthpieces were so treated, these being selected by your Medical Officer from Callboxes in different parts of the City. The result of these experiments was exceedingly satisfactory, and showed that in none of these mouthpieces were any tubercle bacilli present, and, with the exception of one guinea-pig in one experiment, no other microbe pathogenic to the guinea-pig. The exception was an accidental one, and it is obvious that if a sufficiently large number of samples were taken some other germ would almost certainly be found. This has, however, no practical bearing on the case. The conclusion, therefore, is that there is no real risk in the use of the telephone at Public Call Offices, and that the alarm that has been raised has no foundation in fact. It is satisfactory to be assured that the use of so valuable a means of communication is without any attendant danger, and that the only precaution advisable is the obvious common-sense one of keeping the mouthpieces clean. PUBLIC TELEPHONE CALL-BOXES. The attention of the National Telephone Company was also directed to the lack of ventilation of their "call-boxes" in general use, and to the unsuitable character of the material with which they were lined. The Company at once took steps to effect improvements in this direction, and through their courtesy your Medical Officer was afforded an opportunity of viewing and advising upon a specimen of the improved cabinets they propose to erect in future. In the new pattern ample ventilation is provided by means of an opening two feet by six inches in the roof, and by an ingenious mechanical arrangement this is always open except when the instrument within is in actual use, the opening and closing of the tightly-fitting door ensuring free circulation of air throughout. A hard smooth impervious material painted in enamel has been used for lining the interior. As this can be readily cleansed, it is a great improvement upon the former method. 40 A specimen of one of these cabinets was exhibited at the recent Electrical Exhibition, and the Company are to be congratulated upon the prompt and effective manner in which they have dealt with a matter directly connected with the health of their customers. Through the courtesy of the Postmaster-General, I also had an opportunity of examining at the Post Office Factory, Mount Pleasant, the pattern telephone cabinet adopted for the Post Office telephone service, and was able to make some suggestions that might improve their ventilation, &c. ACCOMMODATION OF PATIENTS NOT SUFFERING FROM INFECTIOUS DISEASE IN METROPOLITAN ASYLUMS BOARD HOSPITALS. In September last a communication was received from the Town Clerk of Holborn, stating that the Council of the Metropolitan Borough of Holborn had had under consideration the reports of the Metropolitan Asylums Board for the past four years, and that their attention had been drawn to the fact that during that time no less than 10,000 patients had been admitted suffering from diseases for which the Board's Hospitals were not provided, and that they had been detained on an average about three weeks at a total cost for the whole period of about £60,000, and that having regard to these figures he was directed to ascertain whether, in the view of other Metropolitan Boroughs interested, a joint conference on the subject, between the Metropolitan Boroughs and the Asylums Board, could with advantage be held to discuss the matter. Amongst the suggestions that had been made was one that the Medical Officer of Health should be required to confirm the diagnosis of the medical practitioner in each case. The Corporation were also asked whether they would be prepared, in the event of a conference being decided upon, to appoint representatives. The matter was considered by the Sanitary Committee on the 24th October, when it was decided that no action should be taken in the matter. In view of the sweeping statements contained in the letter in question, it was hardly likely that the Asylums Board would let such a charge go unchallenged, and on the 16th November they issued the following copy of a report which the Metropolitan Asylums Board had received from their Hospitals Committee upon the subject of the circular letter addressed to them by the Holborn Borough Council referred to above:— "METROPOLITAN ASYLUMS BOARD. "Board Meeting (11th November, 1905). "Copy Report of Hospitals Committee. 12nd November, 1905. "Accommodation of Patients.— On the 14th October last, the Board referred to us for "report a letter from the Council of the Metropolitan Borough of Holborn, dated 18th "September, 1905, in which they call attention to an alleged expenditure amounting, 41 "according to their estimate, to about £60,000, which they say has been occasioned "by the admission during the last five years into the Board's Hospitals of some 10,000 cases not suffering from diseases for which these hospitals are provided, and suggest a "conference between the Board and the several Metropolitan Councils to consider the "subject. "As regards the financial statement the Holborn Borough Council seems to have "been misled. The Accountant of the Board has estimated that the extra cost to "which the Board has been put by the admission of these patients would not exceed "an average sum of £3,200 per annum. "As regards the matter which it is proposed to discuss, it is undeniable that in the "earlier stages of many cases of infectious disorder there can be no absolute certainty of diagnosis, and that to detain at home every patient until such certainty is obtained "must frequently result in the spread of infection, the prevention of which is the "primary object of the Board's hospitals. "It must, therefore, be always to remove a doubtful case than to leave it outside "with the danger of infecting others, which if done would of necessity increase the "number of admissions and thus cause a very considerable expenditure of the rates. "The business of the Managers is to receive cases which are certified by a qualified "practitioner, provide proper accommodation, and then leave them in the experienced "hands of the Medical Superintendents. "In our opinion, the Managers would be going quite beyond the scope of their "functions if they took any step which would have the effect of discouraging medical "practitioners from freely exercising their judgment in seeking for their patients "admission to the Board's hospitals. "The suggestion that the Medical Officer of Health should confirm the diagnosis "is not one for the Board; but in our opinion it is very doubtful whether such a "proceeding would be of any value, and it certainly would be attended with "distinct disadvantages. "We do not see that any useful purpose would be served by such a conference as "the Borough of Holborn suggest, and we cannot recommend the Managers to take "any part therein. "We recommend:— "That the report (2nd November, 1905) of the Hospitals Committee on the "subject of the letter (18th September, 1905) from the Council of the "Metropolitan Borough of Holborn with reference to accommodation of patients "be approved and adopted, and a copy thereof forwarded to the Town Clerk in "reply to such communication. "(Adopted.)" As many of the Borough Councils in the Metropolis evidently agreed with the views of the Asylums Board on the subject, in the result a letter, dated the 31st January of this year, was received from the Holborn Borough Council, stating that in reply to their communication of the 18th September, 1905, seventeen Councils were in favour of the conference being held, and nine were against it, they had therefore come to the conclusion not to convene the conference, and that in these circumstances they had asked the Metropolitan Borough Councils' Association to undertake the consideration of the matter. 42 CEREBRO-SPINAL FEVER. A circular letter of the Local Government Board, dated 24th August, 1905, dealing with this disease, was referred to your Medical Officer of Health by the Sanitary Committee for consideration and report. The letter stated that the Board "have reason to believe that the fact that "Cerebro-spinal Fever has been recently somewhat extensively prevalent in "Central Europe and in America has given rise to a doubt, in some quarters, "whether the disease in question may not have newly extended to or have "been developing in this country. The Board think it desirable, therefore, "to state that, so far as they have been able to ascertain, there is no ground "for such apprehension Nevertheless, they consider that "Sanitary Authorities should be on the alert to detect the presence of the "disease in their districts, or to satisfy themselves as to its absence, and to "this end have issued a Memorandum which has been prepared by their "Medical Officer, dealing generally with the characteristic symptoms of the "disease, and especially with its minor and anomalous manifestations." The Board having suggested that there would be advantage in the local Medical Officer of Health endeavouring to secure, by arrangement with the medical men practising in his district, information as to the existence of cases of the kind in question, copies of the Memorandum referred to were forwarded to all practitioners in the City of London, with the following covering letter:— "Corporation of London, "Guildhall, E.C., "11th November, 1905. "Dear Sir, "Cerebro - Spinal Fever. "I beg to enclose copy of a Memorandum issued by the Local Government "Board, pointing out the characteristic symptoms and the minor and anomalous "manifestations of the above disease. "As an aid to diagnosis, should a suspected case occur in your practice, if you "will communicate with me, I will arrange for an examination of the cerebro"spinal fluid for the diplococcus of Weichselbaum, by the Corporation Bacteriologist, "should you so desire it. "Yours faithfully, "W. COLLINGRIDGE, "Medical Officer of Health for the City of London." MEMORANDUM ON CEREBRO-SPINAL FEVER. Cerebro-spinal fever, known also as epidemic cerebro-spinal meningitis, spotted fever, and by many other less frequently used names, has recently attracted renewed attention in this country by reason of the serious mortality occasioned by disease of this class in New York and in certain localities on the Continent of 43 Europe. Interest in this malady has been farther stimulated by the public notice which has been taken of the recent identification of a case in the neighbourhood of London, and of several cases, four of which terminated fatally, at Irthlingborough in Northamptonshire. Cerebro-spinal fever is not of [rare occurrence in the United Kingdom. In the course of the last forty years this malady is known to have been prevalent in a considerable number of different localities in England and Wales, several of these local outbreaks having taken place in recent years. In some instances they have formed the subject of investigation by a medical Inspector of the Local Government Board, whose report has usually been made public. Occurrences of cerebro-spinal fever have also been observed in Scotland and in Ireland. There is, indeed, as will appear later, reason for belief that this disease is even less uncommon in this country than the foregoing particulars would seem to indicate. The late Mr. Netten Radcliffe defined cerebro-spinal fever as "an acute, epidemic disease, characterised by profound disturbance of the central nervous system, indicated at the onset chiefly by shivering, intense headache or vertigo, or both, and persistent vomiting: subsequently by delirium, often violent, alternating with somnolence or a state of apathy or stupor: an acutely painful condition with spasm— sometimes tetanoid— of certain groups of muscles, especially the posterior muscles of the neck, occasioning retraction of the head; and an increased sensitiveness of the surface of the body. Throughout the disease there is marked depression of the vital powers; not unfrequently collapse: and in its course an eruption of vesicles, petechial or purpuric spots, or mottling of the skin, is apt to occur. If the disease tend to recovery, the symptoms gradually subside without any critical phenomena, and convalescence is protracted: if to a fatal termination, death is almost invariably preceded by coma. After death the enveloping membranes of the brain and spinal cord are found in a morbid state, of which the most notable signs are engorgement of the blood vessels, usually excessive, and an effusion of seropurulent matter into the meshes of the pia mater and beneath the arachnoid." To the clinical manifestations of the disease, indicated in this definition, may be added the presence of Kernig's sign* and of tache cérébrale.† Local prevalence of a malady distinguished by the foregoing features would, no doubt, attract attention and would, it may be presumed, lead to early recognition of its true nature. But, while these features are characteristic of cerebro-spinal fever of typical and severe sort, experience has taught us that this fever may and does appear in milder or in anomalous forms which render identification difficult, and which lead to its being mistaken for other ailments of more common occurrence in this country. Illustration of this is afforded by certain localised outbreaks of cerebrospinal fever in the eastern counties in 1890, where this disease was generally mistaken for sunstroke or for enteric fever, or was looked upon as a new form of illness; by the prevalence of what would seem to have been cerebro-spinal fever in Northamptonshire in 1890-91, where the malady was for the most part diagnosed * To obtain this sign, the patient is placed so that the hip-joint is semi-flexed, and while the hip-joint is retained in this position, the knee-joint is extended passively. A contraction of the hamstrings then sets in, which causes resistance to the extension. † A congested streak produced by drawing the finger-nail gently over the patient's thigh, abdomen, or face. In cases of meningitis this appears earlier (within 30 seconds), lasts longer (8, 10, or 15 minutes), and is broader and of deeper colour than in healthy persons. 44 as pneumonia or as sore throat; and by the occurrence of cerebro-spinal fever in Irthlingborongh in the present year, where many of the persons attacked were regarded as suffering from influenza. In these anomalous forms of cerebro-spinal fever, many or even most of the symptoms associated with the recognised type of the disease may be absent, while, in mild cases, they may be so slight or of such brief duration as to escape notice. It is, however, for such cases that it is necessary to be on the outlook, whether in relation with a definite occurrence of cerebro-spinal fever in a locality, or by reason of the prevalence in a particular neighbourhood of illness not clearly referable to definable cause. In these circumstances, there would be advantage in the local medical officer of health endeavouring to secure, by arrangement with the medical men practising in his district, information as to the existence of cases of the kind in question. Failure to recognise cerebro-spinal fever is also apt to happen when the malady is of the "fulminant" variety, in which death ensues rapidly. In these instances the disease has been mistaken for typhus fever, idiopathic tetanus, or malignant measles. An important aid to diagnosis may be found in examination of the cerebro-spinal fluid, withdrawn from the lower part of the spinal canal by lumbar puncture, for the presence of the "diplococcus meningitidis intracellulars" of Weichselbaum; a micro-organism which is now generally regarded as the specific cause of cerebrospinal fever. Whether cerebro-spinal fever is spread by direct infection from person to person is matter of uncertainty; indeed, there is as yet no definite knowledge as to the way or ways in which its transmission may take place. Since, however, the possibility of direct personal infection cannot, on the evidence available, be excluded, it will be wise to endeavour to secure, as far as practicable, the isolation of the sick from the healthy. It will also be advisable to apply suitable measures of disinfection to premises that have been occupied by the sick, and to articles that may have been in relation with them. In view of the fact that the presence of Weichselbaum's diplococcus has been observed in the mucus of the nose and mouth, not only of the sick, but also of those attending on the sick, there may be advantage in resorting to periodical ablutions of the nasal and buccal passages of the sick and their attendants. W. H. POWER, Local Government Board, Medical Officer. July, 1905. ANTHRAX. Two cases of this disease were reported during the year. On the 13th May the Home Office reported that a man employed at a wholesale skin warehouse in the City had died from Anthrax in the London Hospital two days previously. On investigation it was ascertained that the man, who resided at Walthamstow, was taken ill on the 8th May, and admitted on the following day to the London Hospital, where he died on the 11th. 45 The premises where he had been employed were inspected throughout, and many of the skins— Australian Opossum and American Wolf— that it was stated the deceased might have handled were examined, but in the absence of any means of identifying those that may have conveyed the infection, a special search for the bacillus was not made. In the second case notification was received from the Home Office on the 13th that a market clerk employed by a firm of furriers in the City was under treatment for Anthrax at Guy's Hospital. Upon enquiry it was ascertained that the infection did not originate in the City. The patient had been employed inspecting hides on the wharves on the South side of the Thames, the hides coming from all parts of the world, especially from Morocco and the Black Sea, and were of every description. He had been at business during the early part of the week, and on Tuesday, the 9th January, complained of illness and went home. On Friday, the 12th, he was removed to Guy's Hospital, and at once operated upon. Daily reports were received as to his condition, and the patient made good recovery. On 10th October, 1905, the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries issued a Notice giving a brief summary of the symptoms of Anthrax in animals, and calling attention to the danger incurred in slaughtering any animal that appears to be ill, from an unknown cause, and the precautions that should be taken in dressing such an animal after slaughter. The following is the text of the Notice. It is addressed to butchers, slaughterers, knackers, and other persons engaged in Great Britain in slaughtering animals, or in dressing or otherwise handling carcasses of animals:— "ANTHRAX. "Cases come under the notice of the Board of Agriculture and "Fisheries from time to time of persons having contracted Anthrax "whilst engaged in slaughtering animals, or in dressing or otherwise "handling the carcasses of animals. In connection with inquiries made "under the Diseases of Animals Acts, 1894 to 1903, into outbreaks of "Anthrax, it appears from the Reports of the Board's Inspectors as "regards the outbreaks which occurred in Great Britain during the "period from the beginning of July to the end of December of last year, "that as many as twelve persons are known to have contracted the "disease whilst so employed, and that six of these persons died, whilst "in one case amputation of the arm became necessary. "The Board, therefore, think it is desirable to warn persons against "shedding the blood of an animal which appears to be ill from some "unknown cause, and also against cutting or handling the carcass or 46 "skin of any animal which has died suddenly, until careful inquiries "have been made with a view to see whether or not any symptoms* of "Anthrax had been exhibited during life. Should there appear to be "any reason to suspect the presence of Anthrax, it is very desirable "that the slaughter of the animal or the dressing of the carcass "should not be commenced until steps have been taken to investigate "the cause of illness or death, and that the owner should be warned of "the grave risk to human life which might without such investigation "be incurred. "If the course above indicated be followed in suspicious or doubtful "instances, material assistance would thus be rendered to the respective "Authorities concerned in dealing with this very dangerous disease in " man and in animals. "* The symptoms of Anthrax may be summarised as follows:— The disease "shows itself suddenly. It is very fatal, usually within 48 hours. It does not "often in the United Kingdom spread with rapidity from animal to animal, but it "may affect a number o£ swine at the same time if they have been fed on anthrax "flesh or organs. A beast which a short time before appeared to be well is found "dead or in a dying condition. Frequently blood oozes from the nostrils and the "anus. In cattle there are no typical symptoms, but in horses and pigs the region "of the throat is often found to be swollen. "After death the carcass is swollen. Blood is found around the nostrils and "anus. The muscles are often infiltrated with blood at certain points. The lungs "and glands are congested. The spleen is very much enlarged; it is softer and "darker than normal, and its substance usually resembles tar." Instructions were given for a copy of this Notice to be posted in each slaughterhouse in the City. SPREAD OF INFECTIOUS DISEASE BY VAGRANTS. The consideration of this question was initiated in October, 1904, when your Medical Officer was appointed to represent the Corporation at a Conference held at Spring Gardens under the auspices of the London County Council. The meeting was convened with the view of determining how best to prevent the spread of Small-pox throughout the country by vagrants. The Sanitary Committee have been acquainted from time to time with the details of the deliberations both of the Conference and of the Committee to whom active measures were delegated, and the following is a summary of the whole proceedings, which terminated in June, 1905, with no other result than the sympathetic reception by a Minister, now out of office, of a deputation representing the Conference, and associations of public bodies interested in the subject. 47 The Conference met on 10th November, 1904, and was attended by representatives of the following bodies:— The City of London. The London County Council. Twenty-eight County Councils of England and Wales. Fifty-three County Boroughs of England and Wales. Twenty-six Metropolitan Borough Councils. The Metropolitan Asylums Board. The total number of representatives present was 189. The following Resolutions were adopted by the Conference:— "GENERAL MEASURES FOR THE PREVENTION OF THE SPREAD "OF INFECTIOUS DISEASE BY VAGRANTS. "(1) (a) That this Conference of urban and sanitary authorities of England and "Wales recognises the increasing amount of habitual vagrancy as the cause of "wide-spread and disastrous consequences to the public health, and is of opinion "that much more effective measures than are at present adopted should be taken for "preventing the spread of infectious disease by vagrants, and for effectually dealing "with this great and growing danger. "(b) That the evil can only be met by conferring further powers upon the local "authorities, viz., the sanitary authority, the board of guardians, and the magistracy. "(2) That means should be provided for the detention and isolation of any "vagrant found wandering in a public place, if reasonably suspected of being liable "to convey infectious disease. (3) That this Conference is of opinion that it is desirable that— "(a) Parliamentary powers should be sought for the compulsory vaccination and "re-vaccination of all vagrants unable to produce proof of being sufficiently "protected against small-pox on entering casual wards or common lodging-houses, "who, in the opinion of the sanitary authority, have been exposed to the infection "of small-pox, and also that sanitary authorities should have power to grant such "compensation as they think necessary to persons vaccinated or re-vaccinated at "their request who may be prevented on that account from work; "(b) It should be an offence to withhold information or make false statements to "the sanitary authority in carrying out its powers with respect to the disease. "(4) That in order to deal effectually with the transmission of notifiable infectious "disease by tramps, power should be given to county councils to enforce the "existing laws in all cases where sanitary authorities in the county refuse or neglect "to do so. "(5) That the Local Government Board should obtain powers to secure weekly "returns of all cases of infectious disease from all central authorities throughout the "country, and circulate the same. J ' "(6) That it is desirable that in districts comprising groups of counties and "county boroughs, intelligence bureaux should be established, to which information 48 "should be sent from sanitary authorities and workhouses in the district, of persons "of the wandering class who have been exposed to the infection of small-pox; and "that a printed copy of such information should be distributed from the bureau to "every sanitary authority and board of guardians in the district, and that the "expense of working the bureau should be met by contributions from the county "councils and county boroughs forming the district. "(7) That this Conference is of opinion that the port sanitary authorities should "be authorised to take more stringent precautions to prevent the importation of "disease by persons arriving by vessel at the ports who are suffering from small-pox, "or who have been exposed to infection by that disease. "COMMON LODGING-HOUSES AND CASUAL WARDS. "(8) That the local authority should have increased control over common lodging"houses, their keepers, and occupants. Thus the Local Authority should have "power— "(a) to medically examine the inmates; "(6) to detain and isolate persons exposed to infection and to disinfect them and "their clothes; "(c) to temporarily close a common lodging-house in whole or in part, compen"sation to be given to the keeper of the house. "(9) That the local authority should have power to order the keeper of a common "lodging-house in which there has been infectious disease to refuse fresh admissions "for such time as may be required by the authority. "(10) That the local authority shall be empowered to require medical examination "and disinfection of all persons entering casual wards. "(11) That the local sanitary authority should have power to require the removal "and isolation of any inmate of a casual ward who may reasonably be suspected of "being liable to convey infectious disease. "(12) That the local authority should have full power to require the cleansing of "the person and the disinfection of the clothes of any person in a casual ward, "whether infected or exposed to infection. "LABOUR BUREAUX. "(13) (a) That the time has arrived when the Local Government Board should "promote legislation for the establishment of labour bureaux in the areas of every "county council and every county borough council. "(b) That this Conference is also of opinion that it is desirable that a national "voluntary agency should be formed for assisting bona fide working men while "travelling through the country in search of work, and that such agency should be "worked on the same lines as the Inter-Cantonal Union of Switzerland for the "relief of poor travellers. "(14) That the unemployed travelling bona fide in search of work, not being "habitual vagrants, should not be treated as vagrants, but as far as possible be "assisted to obtain employment. 49 " LABOUR COLONIES. "(15) That the time has arrived when the Local Government Board should " promote legislation for the establishment of labour colonies for the compulsory " detention of habitual vagrants until they have acquired power to work and " self.constraint. "(16) That this Conference approves and affirms the resolutions passed by the " executive council of the Association of Poor Law Unions in England and " AVales at their meeting in September, 1903, as follows :— "(a) That boards of guardians should be grouped in suitable areas to provide " labour colonies for vagrants. "(b) That such colonies should be established and governed by the joint " committees of the boards of guardians of the unions in the said areas. " (c) That habitual vagrants should be sent to such labour colonies by magistrates. "(d) That the expenses of each colony should be spread over the unions in " the areas served by such colonies. "CHILDREN OF VAGRANTS. (17) That the powers of the poor law guardians under the Poor Law Acts " of 1889 and 1899 to assume and exercise, in certain circumstances, parental " rights over the children of pauper parents, should be extended so as to confer " similar rights over the children of habitual vagrant " GENERALLY. "(18) That, in order to give practical effect to the foregoing resolutions, this " Conference is in favour of uniting with the County Councils' Association, the " Association of Municipal Corporations, the Association of Metropolitan Borough " Councils, the Magistracy, and the Association of Poor Law Unions of England "and Wales for the following purposes, viz.:—(a) To enlist the sympathy and " co.operation of members of Parliament ; (b) to appoint representatives to wait " on the Local Government Board ; (c) to give evidence before the Inter. " Departmental Committee of the Government on the Vagrancy Question ; (d) to " take such other measures as may be thought desirable in support of the " resolutions arrived at by the Conference. " That a Committee be appointed to give effect to this resolution. "That the Committee do consist of the following members :— " Four to represent the County Councils, seven to represent the County Borough " Councils. For the Corporation of London and Metropolitan Borough Councils, " your Medical Officer and three other members. The Committee to have power " to add to their number." The fact being established that Small.pox is largely spread by vagrants, it is evident that additional powers are necessary for dealing with an evil which necessarily increases with the extension of habitual vagrancy. 50 The general measures proposed by the Conference seem well adapted for this purpose, and it is to be hoped that in spite of the opposition of fanatical anti.vaccinationists, Parliamentary powers will be obtained for the compulsory vaccination and re.vaccination of all vagrants insufficiently protected against Small.pox. Common lodging.houses and casual wards are admittedly frequently foci of disease, and the increased control over them in the direction indicated in the resolution would further lesson the extension of Small.pox. The establishment of Intelligence Bureaux would also be most valuable as going far towards checking the spread of the disease. With regard to the establishment of Labour Bureaux and Labour Colonies, in reporting thereon to the Sanitary Committee, I preferred not to express any opinion, the subject being really one of Poor Law administration, and therefore hardly within a Medical Officer's province. The assumption by the guardians of parental rights over the children of habitual vagrants was also a matter I preferred not to discuss for similar reasons. When the Committee appointed by the Conference met on the 15th December, 1904, your Medical Officer of Health stated the position of the Sanitary Committee with reference to compensation for vaccination in the case of habitual vagrants, and it was understood that as the delegates had no definite instructions to pledge their respective authorities, it would be advisable to rely upon the weight of the Congress, and make it clearly understood that the various authorities were not bound by all the resolutions. (This would cover the objection as to compensation for compulsory vaccination in the cases referred to.) It was generally felt that in the state of public business at the time, a Private Bill, backed by both sides of the House and approved by the Local Government Board, would stand a better chance of becoming law than a Government Bill, and it was therefore agreed that the alternative, i.e., public or private, should be placed before Mr. Long by a deputation. The Chairman further pointed out that the delay in the issue of the report of the Vagrancy Committee would obviously stand in the way of immediate public legislation. After some discussion it was finally agreed that a deputation should wait upon the President of the Local Government Board— (1) To lay before him the Resolutions of the Conference ; (2) To urge in the first place the public health point of view (i.e., the connection between infectious disease and vagrancy), while pointing out the importance of the Labour Bureaux and Labour Colony question as an essential part of the problem. (3) To point out that while certain remedies could be at once applied by the Local Government Board, either by new regulations or by 51 compelling compliance with those already issued, others would require legislation. (4) To offer expert evidence on the question to the Vagrancy Committee. It was resolved that such deputation should consist of members of the Committee only, and that a letter should be addressed to the County Councils of England and Wales, the Corporation of the City of London, the Councils of County Boroughs and Metropolitan Boroughs, the Metropolitan Asylums Board, and to the Clerks of the Peace of the Quarter Sessions, requesting them to take such individual action as they are able within their own districts. It was not until 22nd June last year that arrangement could be made for a deputation, consisting of the Committee of the Conference and representatives from the Association of Poor Law Unions, to be received by the President of the Local Government Board. The deputation, introduced by Sir Walter Plummer, J.P., was received by Mr. Balfour at the House of Commons. Alderman Dr. Newton of Newcastle.upon.Tyne submitted the resolutions adopted by the Conference, and similar resolutions adopted by the Association of Municipal Corporations and of Metropolitan Borough Councils and the Association of Poor Law Unions of England and Wales. He urged that legislation should be promoted at an early date to give effect to the views of the Conference. The Mayor of Wandsworth spoke on behalf of the Metropolitan Authorities, and Dr. G. Millner Rhodes on behalf of the County Councils. At the conclusion of the latter's remarks Mr. Balfour, who gave the deputation a most sympathetic reception, invited all those present to express their views on the question. CLOSURE OF SCHOOLS. In the course of the year three cases occurred where it was necessary to issue formal orders for closure of schools to prevent extension of infectious disease. ST. BARTHOLOMEW THE GREAT SCHOOLS—INFANTS' DEPARTMENT. On the 30th January, 1905, 16 children attending the Infants' Department at St. Bartholomew the Great Schools in Bartholomew Close were attacked with Measles, eight others were excluded on account of disease in the same house, and two were suspected, a total of 26 out of 50 scholars on the roll. In order to comply with the requirements of the Education Board, two Members of the Sanitary Committee, acting upon my advice, signed a formal order directing the closure of the Infant School for fourteen days from the 30th January, 1905. During the closure the school buildings were disinfected and cleansed, and no further extension of the outbreak occurred. 52 SIR JOHN CASS INFANT SCHOOL—MITRE STREET, ALDGATE. The above.mentioned school was closed by order for fourteen days from the 7th April, 1905, in consequence of an outbreak of Measles, it having been ascertained on the previous day that 18 children had been attacked and that in nine cases children were excluded from the school owing to the disease having occurred in their homes. Thus, 27 out of 60 scholars on the roll were absent. The usual cleansing and disinfection of the school buildings was effected during the closure, and no further cases were reported. GRAVEL LANE BOARD SCHOOL. On the 19th May, 1905, it was reported that 32 of the children attending the Infants' Department of the Gravel Lane Board School had been attacked with Measles, that in 16 cases children had been excluded on account of the disease having occurred in their homes, and that 22 other children were kept away on account of Ophthalmia, Ringworm, &c. Thus, 70 out of 360 on the roll were absent In the circumstances I advised the issue of an order, as in the previous case, requiring the closure of the Infants' Department for fourteen days from the 19th May ; in the interval the usual disinfection and cleansing of the school buildings were carried out. Subsequent to the closing of this department, cases of Measles, Ophthalmia, Ringworm, Favus and Scabies occurred in the other departments of the school, and this, together with the fact that 42 of the children were necessarily absent on account of infectious disease occurring in the other persons of the household, occasioned some alarm among the teachers and others as to the advisability of closing the school altogether. Though there did not seem to me to be any reason for this, it appeared to be wise to have the whole school thoroughly disinfected. I therefore called upon the Medical Officer of the Education Department of the London County Council to have this carried out. The school was closed for the purpose from the 29th May to the 3rd June. During this interval the whole of the buildings were sprayed with a solution of Cyllin (the routine method of dealing with cases of infected rooms in the City), and the books and materials disinfected. I subsequently called the attention of the Medical Officer to certain points which were unsatisfactory with regard to the school generally, viz.:— 1.—The staff provided for cleansing is too small to secure it being thoroughly carried out. This is essential in all schools, especially where Ringworm, Favus and Ophthalmia have appeared, the spread of which is effected by the presence of dust. 2.—In view of the practical impossibility of preventing the use of towels in common with a large number of children, that it is advisable that the lavatories should be locked during such time as there are any cases of these diseases in the school, and any necessary use of such accommodation made only under personal supervision, so as to prevent the spread of infection by towels. 53 3.—In order to minimise the risk of infection by handling books in common, it was suggested that the material should be allotted to individual scholars, and that for this purpose each set of books should be enclosed in a separate bag of such material as can be easily and readily cleansed. The expense of this would be small. 4.—With a view to minimise the amount of dust in the school, the drilling should be carried on out of doors or in a covered shed, and if at any time this was found to be impracticable, instructions should be given to prevent stamping on the floor during such exercises, as observations have shown that this is productive of dust. 5.—Attention was called to the insanitary construction of the latrine closets at present in use. The Medical Officer in reply wrote stating that "the defective sanitation has been reported to the Architect, and will be attended to." With the exception of Nos. 1 and 2 the above recommendations have been carried out. CREMATION. The principal objection to this—the most sanitary method of disposal of the dead—is entirely one of sentiment. Since the passing of the Cremation Act of 1902 the chief cause of opposition, apart from sentimentalism, was based upon the opportunity it might afford for the destruction by fire of evidence of foul play in cases of murder. This has now been entirely eliminated. The Cremation Act directs that cremation can only be carried out under regulations issued by the Home Secretary, and the regulations now in force are so carefully drawn up that—while they do not impose any unreasonable restrictions—the chances of any person who caused death maliciously being able to escape justice are now less than in cases when bodies are interred in the usual manner. This will be made clear by reference to the regulations themselves. It is scarcely necessary to discuss here the serious objections attaching to the system of earth burial now obtaining. They are many, and its abandonment for a method that is without danger to the living will inevitably be forced upon the public. Firstly, intramural burial was prohibited ; next, burial grounds in thickly populated districts have one by one been closed. When it is remembered that some 735,000 bodies have annually to be disposed of in England and Wales every year, it is not surprising to find that these difficulties arise— London alone contributes between seventy and eighty thousand of these bodies, and the time must come when the question of earth burial will become acute. It is not only insanitary, but expensive, and any method that provides the poorer classes with a cheaper mode of dealing with their dead should be 54 welcomed by them. Prejudice for many 'years has to a great extent been responsible for the slow growth of cremation in this country ; there are, however, clear indications of its coming into greater favour. That such is the case is shown by the following tabulated statement giving the number of bodies cremated annually since the Woking installation was established in 1885.. The several towns where Crematoria are erected are shown, and the date of opening them indicated in the first column. Thirteen Crematoria are now in operation in this country, four of which were opened during 1905. CREMATIONS IN THE UNITED KINGDOM, 21 Years—1885.1905. YEAR. Woking. Manchester. Glasgow. Liverpool. "Viscount Cross in the year 1879 forbid the practice of cremation there (Woking) under the threat of " passing an Act to make it illegal .... But in 1884 appeared the well.known judgment of Mr. Justice " Stephen, which, in the case of a child's body having been burned by its father contrary to the order of the " coroner, determined cremation to be a legal act, provided no nuisance be caused thereby to others."—Extract from speech made by the late Sir Henry Thompson at the opening of Golder's Green Crematorium, 22nd November, 1902. City of London Crematorium, IIford. 55 The chart, p. 54, shows in a graphic manner the marked increase of recent years in the proportion of cremations to deaths occurring in the United Kingdom. Reference is made in the foregoing remarks to the sentimental aspect of the question, and, unfortunately, while a great number of persons—probably the majority—express their sympathy with this method of burial reform, when death occurs their friends do not follow out the wishes of the deceased, in many cases because they shrink from a procedure of which they have at present but little information. As an illustration showing how familiarity with cremation dispels prejudice against it, the Manager of the Manchester Crematorium states that "out of " ninety.seven cremations in 1905, no fewer than sixty.eight were those of " persons who had resided in the immediate neighbourhood of the Crema. " torium. This important fact demonstrates that it is only necessary for a " true knowledge of cremation to become more widely spread to ensure its " more general adoption." There is one point with regard to cremation which should be placed very clearly before the working classes. Not only is it cheaper than interment, but there is only one method of procedure when cremation takes place, and that, therefore, all classes receive exactly the same treatment. The Honorary Secretary of the Scottish Burial Reform and Cremation Society, in reply to my enquiry as to the trend of public opinion, says : " Since I first came in touch with this movement, about sixteen years ago, " there is no doubt that the abhorrance and repugnance with which the public " originally regarded the subject has almost entirely disappeared. ... I am " quite convinced that, as time goes on, cremation will be more and more " adopted, although its universal adoption is out of the question." It is important that the sympathy of undertakers should be enlisted in the movement, as there is no question as to the great influence they are able to exercise in the question of disposal of the dead. In this connection the Superintendent of the Hull Crematorium remarks: "I am afraid it will be " some time before cremation will make much progress, unless we can get the " undertakers more in favour of it." Several cases I have heard of, " the " undertaker has persuaded the friends against it when they have been a bit " doubtful." The Manager of the Leicester Crematorium thinks "that in the near future " cremation will become much more popular than at present, more particularly " if it is adopted by the upper class as a means of disposing of the dead (and " there seems to be a leaning in that direction), then the middle classes " (upper and lower) are almost sure to ape them in this as in most things." The Manager of the Birmingham Crematorium is exceedingly sanguine, he considers "the prejudice which exists is gradually lessening, and that in a few " years cremation will be a general mode of the disposal of the dead." 56 The Superintendent of the Leeds Crematorium is of opinion "that the "prejudice against cremation is lessening, though dying hard. Personally I " Believe that there is an appreciable tendency towards this form of disposal of "the dead, and one hears repeated expressions of satisfaction that a Crema"torium has been provided in this neighbourhood. The near future may not "see any great change in the sentiment and opinion of the general public "towards cremation, but I incline towards the belief that the next generation "will accept cremation more as a custom and less as an innovation, the feeling " against it having to be banished by education and familiarity." ARTIZANS' DWELLINGS, STONEY LANE, HOUNDSDITCH. These buildings, erected by the late Commissioners of Sewers and opened in January, 1885, stand upon ground formerly the site of several courts and alleys in the Ward of Portsoken. There are five "blocks" of Dwellings, severally named King's—Queen's— Prince's—North—and South, designed for occupation by persons of the artizan class ; some of the ground floors being let as shops. Exclusive of the shops and cellars, they contain 501 rooms, arranged in 241 tenements, and are occupied by 995 persons. Sixty.one per cent, are adults and thirty.nine per cent, children (under 14 years). The death rate for 1905 was 17.0 per 1,000 per annum [of which 35.3 per cent, was of children under 5 years of age]. Deaths of children under one year to 1,000 births were 138, as against 139 for the City generally. The number of births was 29, viz. : 17 males and 12 females. The birth rate, 29.1 per 1,000, is the lowest recorded since 1894, and is 6.7 per 1,000 below the average for the previous five years. Only one illegitimate birth has been recorded in these Dwellings since 1894, which is consistent with the high reputation for morality among the Jews, by whom they are mainly occupied. In the course of excavations for additional buildings to St. Bartholomew's Hospital on the site of the old Christ's Hospital a large number of human remains were discovered. There is no record as to how or when these were placed in this position, but from the way in which they were grouped it would appear that they had been shot here after removal from some burial ground. A licence was obtained from the Home Office for the removal of these remains, and they were reinterred at the City of London Cemetery at Ilford under the supervision of your Medical Officer of Health. 63 HYGIENE OF HAIRDRESSERS AND BARBERS' SHOPS. Considerable progress has been made during the year in effecting improvements in the conduct of the business of Hairdressers and Barbers within the City, in accordance with the recommendations of the Sanitary Committee approved by the Corporation. Some difficulty was experienced in inducing City Hairdressers to effect registration with the Incorporated Guild of Hairdressers, Wigmakers and Perfumers, it being found that many of them, while willing to make improvements in the conduct of their business, declined to apply for the Certificate through the Incorporated Guild of Hairdressers. Inasmuch as the public health is directly concerned with the sanitation of Barbers' shops and Hairdressers' saloons, it is undesirable that any obstacle should be placed in the way of those willing to conform to the regulations and recommendations adopted by the Corporation. In these circumstances your Medical Officer advised that members of these trades should be notified that the Corporation would grant voluntary Certificates to any Hairdresser within the City boundary whose premises were in a sanitary condition and who would undertake to carry the regulations into effect. This course was agreed to and a form of Certificate adopted. Applications were received from the undermentioned Hairdressers whose establishments were in a satisfactory sanitary condition, and Certificates were issued to them upon their undertaking to comply with the Regulations of the Corporation :— Saulet, Lucien, 5, Eastcheap. " Electra House" (L. Flittner), Finsbury Pavement. The Gresham Toilet Company (E. H. Prince), 93a, Gresham Street. Danziger, Isaac, 8, Aldgate High Street. Schocke, Alexander, 149, Cannon Street. Woodhams, Percy, 17, Lime Street. .Collier, James Astill, 75, Little Britain. Cohen, Samuel, 1 and 2, Snow Hill. Foerstel, Frederick Gustav, 1, Telegraph Street. Gilbert, Percy, 14, Ivy Lane. Heifer, Antoine, 6, King Street, Cheapside. Kreisman, Louise, 21, Liverpool Street. Lehman, Charles, 8 and 9, Aldermanbury Postern. Meinhardt, Gustav Adolph, 120, Cheapside. New Baltic Toilet and General Requisite Company, Limited, £7, St. Mary Axe. * These premises have been closed since registration. 64 Pozzi, Pierre, 16, Lawrence Lane. Schwefel, Carl, 45, Eastcheap. .Szalay, Joseph Page, 8, Cullum Street. Friedel, Franz, 35, Moorgate Street. Hartmann, Carl, 5, Queen's Head Passage, Newgate Street. Krajewski, Gustav, 137, Fenchurch Street. Rudolph, Henry, 5, Crosby Square. A number of other Hairdressers have applied for registration, and are still under consideration. There is a general movement of the City Trade towards increased attention to sanitary matters, and the registration will undoubtedly give a feeling of confidence to those who make use of these establishments ; there is also indication of a growing desire on the part of Hairdressers in the City, and especially those occupying the better class of saloon, to take advantage of the Corporation's scheme of voluntary registration. Cases have occurred where members of the Trade, who at first opposed the movement, now recognise that it is of advantage to fall into line with those who have been placed on the register, particularly where there is close trade competition, even when so doing involves expenditure in carrying out the sanitary requirements. A case heard in the Marylebone County Court on the 1st January last illustrates the importance—both from the customer's and tradesman's point of view—of taking precautions against transmission of disease through the agency of infected implements. The plaintiff in the case sued a barber and hairdresser for damages in respect of his wages, pain, and suffering sustained through an attack of barber's rash, which it was alleged was contracted on the defendant's premises. The Jury awarded the plaintiff 15/. damages and costs. It is not too much to say that a similar cause for action cannot arise in connection with any of the registered Hairdressers in the City, while it is clearly shown by the above case that in unregistered places of business or in those where no proper antiseptic precautions are taken, not only is the customer liable to contract disease, but the proprietor of the business is rendered liable for heavy damages as a consequence. LONDON COUNTY COUNCIL (GENERAL POWERS) ACT, 1905. The Bill, as introduced, contained certain clauses relating to the prevention of Tuberculosis and infectious disease caused by contaminated milk, which were, with certain modifications, a reproduction of clauses introduced for similar purposes in the London County Council (General Powers Bill) for 1902, with regard to which I reported at length to the Sanitary Committee in January, 1905. * These premises have been closed since registration. 65 Powers were asked for the more efficient dealing not only with milk that had been contaminated and likely to cause infectious disease or Tuberculosis in those consuming it, but also with milk found to be for any reason unsound, unwholesome or unfit for the food of man. There was a general consensus of opinion that these former were needed, but there was much difference of opinion as to the administration of them, and the Corporation and the majority of the Borough Councils in the Metropolis petitioned against the County Council being the authority for that purpose as suggested in the Bill. In the passage of the Bill through the Commons an instruction was given to the Police and Sanitary Committees that the powers asked for by the London County Council, which were admitted to be important, should be entrusted to the City and the Metropolitan Borough Councils, but the County Council subsequently withdrew the whole of these provisions, Part V. of the Bill, which was to give power to the London County Council to make representations to the Local Government Board as to the administration of the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, having previously been struck out by resolution of the House.
