These ten Blocks, containing 443 tenements, are inhabited by 1,556 persons = 3.5 persons to each tenement. COMMON LODGING-HOUSES. Of the three common lodging.houses on the Register at the commencement of 1905 two have been closed, the property having been acquired by the Corporation for rebuilding purposes. One other has been placed on the Register, viz., No. 116, Middlesex Street. This building, which has been acquired by the Salvation Army, is a substantial brick structure of the warehouse class formerly used as a boot factory. It is situated near the boundary of Stepney, but within the City precincts. The premises consist of basement, ground, and five upper floors. All floors have been rendered fireproof. The doors of the dormitories are of two. inch teak and open outwards on to stone staircases. The dormitories are well 68 lighted and have through ventilation. The whole building is warmed by hot.water pipes. The drainage, water.closets, lavatory basins, baths, &c., were newly constructed, and the whole of the appointments and fittings are of excellent design. On 27th February of this year your Medical Officer laid before the Sanitary Committee formal certificate with regard to this building as required by Section 10 of the City of London Sewers Act, 1851, and the premises are now registered as a common lodging.house for accommodating 453 persons. The establishment of a common lodging.house of such dimensions within the City will probably result in an increase in the number of persons chargeable upon the City of London Union ; as destitute inmates will be removed to the Infirmary, and will require careful supervision. An important judgment was delivered last year in the case of Parker v. Talbot, which decided that charitable institutions run on common lodging. house lines and where no charge is made are not common lodging.houses, and can therefore be carried on without licence or registration. "HIGH COURT OF JUSTICE.. " COURT OF APPEAL, October 24th, 25th, November 1st. " Before Vaughan Williams, Stirling, and Cozens Hardy, L.JJ. " parker V. talbot. " Common Lodging.House—Registration—No payment. .' If no charge is made to persons who frequent a charitable instihition which is con. " ducted on lines similar to common lodging.houses, such institution is not a " common lodging.house within the meaning of the Common Lodging.IIouses Act, " 1851.1853, and no registration or licence is required. " This was an appeal from a judgment of Kekewich, J., holding that a charitable " institution known as the Providence (Row) Night Refuge and Home was a com. " mon lodging.house, and ought to have registered as such under the Common " Lodging.Houses Act, 1851, and that a licence to carry it on should have been " obtained under the London County Council (General Powers) Act, 1902. The " action was started with the object of obtaining a decision of the Court of Appeal " on the point, as a divisional court had held in Gilbert v. Jones (1905), 2 K.B. 691, " that the institution was a common lodging.house, and there was no appeal from " that decision. Kekewich, J., held in accordance with that decision. " Vaughan Williams, L.J., said he would have decided the case in the same way " as the divisional court had done, were it not for the Common Lodging.Houses " Act (Ireland), 1860. He thought the definition clause in Section 3 of that Act " was meant to apply to the English Acts of 1851.1853. That Section defined the " term ' common lodging.house ' as meaning ' a house in which persons are harboured " ' or lodged for hire for a single night or for less than a week at a time, or any part " ' of which is let for any term less than a week.' People in this institution were " not lodged for hire, and therefore it was not a common.lodging.house. The " attention of the lower courts had not been drawn to this statute. " It was afterwards pointed out to the Court that the Common Lodging.Houses " (Ireland) Act, 1860, had been repealed. The Court held that this made no " difference, as the Act declared what meaning was intended to be put on the " earlier Acts." . " Public Health," January, 1906. 69 FACTORY AND WORKSHOP ACT, 1901. FACTORIES, WORKSHOPS, AND WORKPLACES. This Act came into operation on the 1st January, 1902, but in view of the obligations of the Corporation thereunder, the work of inspection was commenced in the preceding year. The nucleus of the Register of Workshops was compiled from information supplied by H.M. Factory Department from the existing Registers. This, however, was found upon inspection, to be very imperfect, many of the workshops having ceased to exist, and some factories inadvertently having been included. These discrepancies, with others, as, for instance, places originally occupied as " workshops " developing into " factories," have been adjusted, and the Register is now as reasonably complete as the fluctuating character of the trades registered permit. It has been found necessary, in order to ensure accuracy in the record, as well as for the convenience of the Department, that a new Register should be compiled each year, all lapsed workshops being eliminated. The Act defines various classes of workshops, and the Workshop Register is so arranged as to show the nature of the workshops inspected, whether of a general character, or those specially defined in the Act as "Domestic," " Tenement," or " Men's " workshops, the date notified or discovered and inspected, the number of workers employed (whether male or female), and the initials of the officer inspecting. The Register of Workshops for 1905 shows a total of 1,947 premises where workshops exist or have existed during the preceding year. Workshops at 478 premises have ceased to exist, and 612 new premises have been added. It has not been found possible to inspect all the premises registered, so that the total number of workrooms existing cannot be given. Some of the trades have ten or more workshops on the premises. There is no statutory obligation to keep a Register of factories or workplaces, but for the sake of convenience Registers have been adopted in both cases on similar lines to that for workshops, but the registration of workplaces has been confined to kitchens of restaurants, &c.—total number of kitchens registered during the year was 44. The Register of workplaces dates from 1902, that for factories was commenced towards the end of 1904. The total number of workrooms in factories, workshops, and workplaces inspected during the year amounts to 3,443, but this does not represent the total number registered, owing, as before mentioned, to some 330 premises not 70 having been re.inspected. It should be mentioned that railway station and other public lavatories, stable yards, &c., are not included in the above figures, although they come within the definition of " workplace." These places are regularly inspected, although they do not appear in the Factory Act Registers. Barbers' shops are also workplaces, even if they are not workshops, and there are many other occupations where the Act would apply. The inspections of the Women Sanitary Inspectors are included in the above figures, but the details of the work performed appear under a separate heading on page 79. The following is a summary of inspections made annually since the passing of the Act:— Factories. 1904—Inspections during the year 78 1905 ,, ,, ,, 735 813 Workshops. 1901 745 1902 481 1903 547 1904 1,293 1905 2,025 - 5,091 Workplaces. 1902 468 1903 357 1904 93 1905 854 1,772 Totals. Inspections during 1901-5 7,676 ” ” 1905 3,614 FACTORIES. In my last Annual Report I mentioned that but little opportunity had hitherto existed for the inspection of factories. The inspection of factories falls primarily within the scope of the duties of H.M. Factory Department, but Local Authorities also have duties with respect to the sanitary conditions, independently of the Home Office, it being their duty to deal with nuisances existing on any premises. It is the duty of 71 H.M. Factory Inspector to give notice to the Local Sanitary Authority of any nuisance which is not remediable under the Factory and Workshop Act. A list of complaints thus received is dealt with on page 77. With a view of anticipating the action of H.M. Factory Inspectors, a regular inspection of factories within the City was instituted in 1904, and systematically pursued during 1905, with the result that 230 premises have been inspected, having an aggregate of 750 workrooms, an average of over three workrooms to each. The trades engaged in include :— Bookbinding. Bookfolding. Cigarette making. Cigars and tobacco. Cloth working. Costumes. Die sinking. Electrotyping. Engraving. Envelopes. Hatmaking. Machine ruling. Mantle making. Perfumery. Pickling. Printing. Stationery. Stereotyping. Surgical instruments, &c., &c. Speaking generally, the defects found are similar to those found in most workshops on first inspection, viz., want of suitable water.closet accommodation and defective sanitary fittings, also defective and dirty walls, floors, and ceilings. Most of the work before mentioned has been performed independently of any action on the part of H.M. Factory Inspectors, and in no way interferes with the work of that department, except in removing cause for future complaint. It may be of interest to state that in the 230 premises visited there were 39 workrooms in which women were exclusively employed ; 74 where men were so employed, and in the remaining 637 both sexes were engaged. . As mentioned in previous Reports, the sanitary defects generally found in workshops may be classified under the following headings:— Want of cleansing. Insufficient ventilation. Overcrowding. The lack of separate and suitable water-closet accommodation for both sexes. An impression is prevalent that whitewashing and cleansing generally are only required at stated periods, say once in six or twelve months or longer, the real test, viz., whether cleansing is wanted at the particular time, being overlooked. However, but little trouble has been experienced in 73 getting necessary work done when the facts were brought to the knowledge of the parties responsible. Much difficulty is experienced in dealing with the subject of ventilation where the employment of gas rings and stoves, &c., is the polluting agent— in many cases the only real solution in such cases is mechanical ventilation. * Details of Workshop Inspections, 1905. (Underground.) Bakehouses have been the subject of special legislation for many years, and since the 1st January, 1896, it has been unlawful to establish any new one underground. The Factory and Workshop Act of 1901 introduced some stringent measures with regard to these places, and it was therein enacted that after the 1st January, 1904, no existing underground* bakehouse should be used unless certified by the Sanitary Authority to be "suitable as regards construction, light, ventilation, and in all other respects" (Sec. 101 (4)). Early in that year the whole of the underground bakehouses had been dealt with, i.e., were certified as suitable or had permanently ceased to be used as such. The following is a list of the underground bakehouses in the City dealt with under the Factory and Workshop Act, 1901:— 188, Aldersgate Street. 85, Fore Street. 81, Bishopsgate Street Without. †67, Farringdon Street. 59 to 62, „ „ †88, †4, Broadway. †93, Fetter Lane. 95, Cannon Street. † 12, Gracechurch Street. 8, Copthall Avenue. †21, Holborn. 15, Cornhill. 8 to 14, King Street, Snow Hill. 15, Duke Street. 48, Knightrider Street. * An underground bakehouse is one in which the floor surface of any baking room is more than 3 feet below the surface of the footway of the adjoining street, or of the ground adjoining or nearest to the room. † These have been established at least 21 years. 75 8, Lambeth Hill. 3, Pancras Lane. 18, Long Lane. 21, Stoney Lane. †37, Middlesex Street. 68, St. Mary Axe. †63 to 65, „ †75, St. Paul's Churchyard. †l22, ,, 194, Upper Thames Street. 31, Newbury Street. WORKPLACES. (Kitchens of Restaurants, &c.). The inspection of these places was proceeded with as usual, and there is little to add to what has been set forth in former Reports, especially that of last year. In that Report the subject was dealt with in a fairly exhaustive manner from the sanitary standpoint, and some of the paragraphs which might be of value to anyone seeking information on the several points are added as an appendix to this Report (see page 166). The question of suitable larder accommodation has received special attention. In one case, meat was found deposited in what was practically the lobby of a water-closet, the occupier protesting that he had no other place for it; but an application to the owner of the premises resulted in a good safe being provided outside a staircase window. * Established during 1903. † These have been established at least 21 years. 76 Most of the persons spoken to on the subject of larder accommodation state that they have no need for a larder, as they clear out from day to day, and so have nothing to store in the way of perishable food. This is no doubt usually the case, but it is obvious that a suitable larder cannot fail to be required occasionally. In January, 1904, the Corporation adopted a standard of sanitary requirements for kitchens and aboveground bakehouses to be complied with by applicants for Certificates. Up to the present only three Certificates have been granted. Two applications were received during the year, but were not proceeded with. OUTWORKERS. The difficulty of getting employers of outworkers to send in lists as required by the Act still continues. With a view of dealing with defaulters in an effectual manner, all employers of outworkers registered were informed by post of the requirements of the Act, but out of a total of 376 notices issued only 162 firms replied, leaving 214 who either neglected or declined to send lists. It has been decided to give notice to all employers of outworkers, in the future, a short time before the lists become due. This was done early in the present year (1906) with good results. INFECTIOUS DISEASE AT OUTWORKERS' PREMISES. Domestic Workshop. Two cases of Scarlet Fever occurred in the children of an outworker. The family consisted of 13 persons in all, the elder children being, with their parents, engaged in the tailoring trade. The work was stopped immediately, and every precaution taken to prevent the spread of the disease. After disinfection the premises were thoroughly cleansed throughout. 79 WOMEN SANITARY INSPECTORS. Vitiation of the air of the workshop by gas whether used for heating or for lighting continues to be a source of difficulty. Where gas stoves are used a hood and flue are required in order to carry off the injurious products of combustion, and where the gas is used for lighting purposes, the substitution of incandescent for ordinary burners effects a considerable reduction in the vitiation of the air. OUTWORKERS' PREMISES. In the majority of cases the outworkers on the register employ workers other than members of their own families, and their premises are therefore included under the heading "Workshops." There are, besides, twenty-one domestic workshops which have been inspected; with two exceptions, these latter were found to be in a fairly satisfactory condition as regards cleanliness of rooms, &c. Some drainage and structural defects were discovered and were referred to the District Inspector. In the case of one outworker, an alien, who employed three men and two women, the workrooms were found to be in such an extremely dirty condition that the outworkers' employer (a tailor living outside the City) was informed that the place in which the work was being done was in such a condition that no more work should be sent to him until the premises had been cleansed, failing which the matter would be dealt with under Section 108, Factory and Workshop Act, 1901. The employer wrote in reply thanking your Medical Officer for the notification, and stating that no more work would be given to the outworker. In addition to the dirty condition of the workrooms, one was found to be overcrowded, the roof was defective, an accumulation of refuse on an out-house roof gave off very offensive effluvia, which came into the house through the staircase window, the stairs were dirty, the W.C. in the yard was in a filthy condition and was used in common by the persons of both sexes employed. Notices were served on the owner and on the occupier, calling upon them to abate the nuisances and remedy the defects complained of. 81 The owner put the more immediately necessary cleansing in hand, and after some difficulty got rid of the tenant, when the premises were cleansed throughout and the other defects remedied. INDUSTRIAL LAW INDEMNITY FUND. A report of the working of this Fund has been received. It is formed to protect women and young persons of both sexes who are dismissed from their employment for giving truthful evidence to H.M. Inspectors of Factories and Mines, Sanitary Inspectors and Shop Hours Inspectors. Several typical cases are quoted which show that the Fund is administere with discretion and that it does useful work in removing hardships which must often arise in enforcing the provisions of the Factory and Workshop Act, and other laws relating to industrial matters. WORKPLACES (Kitchens). Twelve new kitchens have been inspected and two hundred and eightyseven periodical inspections of those previously visited have been made. The defects found may be grouped under three heads, viz.:— (i.) Want of cleansing. (ii.) Want of ventilation— (a) Gas stoves in kitchens without any effectual means of carrying off the injurious products of combustion. (b) Kitchens otherwise badly ventilated. (iii.) Sanitary conveniences not properly cut off from direct communication with the kitchens or the rooms. In several cases it was found that in order to enlarge the kitchen an adjoining passage had been thrown into it, or some other structural alteration had been made which resulted in establishing a direct communication between the kitchen and the sanitary convenience. The work needed to remedy the defects has necessitated three hundred and ninety-one re-inspections, and is for the most part now completed. DOMESTIC WORKSHOPS AND FACTORIES. "Domestic Workshops" and "Domestic Factories" are places in which no mechanical power is used, and in which the only persons employed are members of the same family dwelling there; but when work is done only at irregular intervals, and does not furnish the whole or the principal means of living to the family, the place ceases to come under the provisions of the Act. Places where the family are engaged either in straw plaiting, pillow lace making, or glove making are also exempt. 82 Eight Domestic Workshops within the City were duly notified to H.M. Thirty-eight offices where lady clerks are employed have been inspected with reference to the sanitary accommodation provided. In thirty cases no separate sanitary convenience was provided, at three premises the accommodation was unsuitable, and at three the conveniences were dirty. One room, in which eleven girls were employed in typewriting, was found to be in a very dirty condition, two rooms were badly ventilated and there were five "general" defects, making a total of forty-four defects in thirty-four offices. In four cases there was no cause for action. Thirty-one defects have already been remedied and the remainder are in hand, with one exception, where the firm were shortly giving up the lease of the premises, and the required re-arrangement of the sanitary accommodation was not pressed. On the whole the owners of premises as well as the employers have shown themselves ready to carry out the suggestions made. LADIES' WAITING ROOMS AT RAILWAY STATIONS. There are twenty-three railway stations in the City at which ladies' waiting rooms are provided. The number of waiting rooms is twenty-nine, containing eighty-eight conveniences. These have been periodically inspected, and a very marked improvement is noticeable in the condition of the lavatories and sanitary conveniences. This is especially the case at Liverpool Street Station, where, on the suggestion of your Medical Officer of Health, new wash-down closets have been substituted for those of the old valve pattern. The latter were a constant source of complaint, but since the new ones have been adopted the conveniences at all the waiting rooms at this station have been reported as being in quite a satisfactory condition. The ventilation of the ladies' waiting rooms at Fenchurch Street (First Class) and at Aldersgate Street has been considerably improved, and at the latter station the conveniences are now lighted naturally, doing away with the gas which was formerly burnt all day. Some minor defects were found at various stations, and these have been remedied. SANITARY ACCOMMODATION FOR WOMEN. In November last your Medical Officer reported to the Streets Committee on the above question as follows:— " There are now eleven underground conveniences for women in the " City, as follows :— " Aldersgate Street (General Post Office). " Aldersgate Street (North end). *" Circus Place. *" Farringdon Street South (Ludgate Circus). 84 " Farringdon Street North. *" Golden Lane. " Holborn. " Minories. " Upper Thames Street. " West Smithfield. " Guildhall Buildings (not yet opened). " Free Accommodation. "At those marked with an asterisk free accommodation is provided " for women only, and at Circus Place for children only. "These conveniences are systematically inspected by your Women "Sanitary Inspectors, and as a rule are found to be kept clean and in "good sanitary condition. "Marked improvement has been effected in some of them by the "fixing of new gas stoves with suitable flues to carry off the products " of combustion, and I understand that shortly all will be thus provided. "Minor defects are reported to Mr. Engineer from time to time and " dealt with by him. "I have now to call your attention to the urgent necessity for "increased free accommodation, and improvement in that already "supplied. "It is not necessary to enter into details as to the serious injury that "may be inflicted upon women by the absence of lavatory accommodation. "Suflice it is to state that serious and life-long trouble is at times the "result of such absence. This has already been recognised and "provision made wherever practicable. "While the ill-results are far more serious in the case of women than "of men for physiological reasons, yet with the exception of the two "places above mentioned, no free accommodation is provided for them, "and unfortunately, as long as payment is required, for many it is beyond "reach. "The largest class of woman for whom free accommodation is needed "in the City is composed of girls who carry materials to and from the "shops and places where the work is given out. "The number of outworkers employed by City firms is considerable "(already more than 7,000 have been registered), and of these only a "very small proportion live in the City. "These outworkers largely employ girls to carry the work to and fro, "and these girls have frequently long distances to traverse, often carrying "heavy weights. The nature of their employment entails upon them "long waiting and need for haste on their return journey. For them no 85 "accommodation is provided at the workshop, and they obviously cannot "afford to pay out of their scanty earnings even the small amount "required for the use of the public convenience, and the present provision "being situated in the Northern and Western Districts only, it is not ''available to many of these women to whom time is money, and who "have but little knowledge of the grave consequences that often result "from neglect of natural functions. "It is necessary that free accommodation be provided for women "at each public convenience, and I recommend that this be at once "arranged for. "Arrangements for Free Accommodation. "At present the closets for free accommodation are provided with "doors without fastenings, or curtains only, to which system there are "many objections. "The attendant has no control as to the number of persons entering "the compartment at once—has no opportunity (where the conveniences "are in constant use) of seeing whether the closet is in proper order — " and in any case of abuse has no means of detecting the offender— "while further, the absence of privacy undoubtedly means unnecessary "discomfort in the case of decent persons who by reason of poverty or "other cause are compelled to seek free accommodation. "I have therefore to recommend that all free conveniences be fitted "with doors (without inside bolts), and that such doors be fastened "with locks under the control of the attendants. "Increased accommodation for women is urgently needed in the "Eastern Districts, Aldgate and Bishopsgate, and I recommend that it "be referred to Mr. Engineer to consider and report as to how this "should be provided." On consideration of the above Report the Committee decided at once to provide free accommodation at each public convenience within the City. It was further resolved to refer to Mr. Engineer to report as to further sanitary accommodation for women in Aldgate and Bishopsgate, and the Court of Common Council subsequently agreed (1st March, 1906) to provide further accommodation for women at the existing underground conveniences in Aldgate High Street and Bishopsgate Street Without at an estimated cost of £1,600. The gas stoves referred to in the foregoing Report have since been removed, and new ones with suitable flues for carrying off the products of combustion substituted. These have effected a marked improvement in the condition of the atmosphere in the attendants' rooms. 86 THIS TABLE WAS ISSUED BY THE LOCAL GOVERNMENT BOARD BY REQUEST OF_THE SECRETARY OF STATE. The year's figures required in the Table are then obtained by adding together the two half-yearly totals. † Number of premises registered (number of workshops not known, the whole of the premises registered not having been re-inspected). 88 FACTORY AND WORKSHOP ACT (1901) AMENDMENT BILL. This Amendment Bill was an attempt by Private Members to give effect, with some slight modification, to the clause affecting laundries of religious or charitable institutions, as provided for in the original draft of the Factory and Workshop Act Amendment Bill of 1901. Under the existing law laundries carried on in religious or charitable institutions by way of trade or for purposes of gain are exempted from the operations of the Factory and Workshop Act. This exemption was made originally in the Factory and Workshop Act, 1895, when laundries were brought within the scope of the Factory Act. The Factory and Workshop Act Amendment Bill, 1901, contained considerable amendments of the provisions relating to laundries, while those of religious and charitable institutions were to be placed under supervision under certain conditions. In Committee, however, so much opposition was made to these proposals that the clause was withdrawn by the Government, and the law of 1895 on the subject was left unaltered. In 1902, in consequence of considerable discussion both in the House of Commons and the House of Lords, the Home Secretary, in a circular letter dated the 24th January of that year, endeavoured to ascertain whether any considerable number of these institutions would be willing to receive visits from Factory Inspectors, and intimated that if such were the case he would instruct these officers to visit them and offer such advice and assistance as might be in their power. He also stated that, if so desired, the visits would be made by Lady Inspectors of the Home Office Staff. The results of this enquiry are summarised in a Parliamentary White Paper issued last year, which conclusively proves that there are a very large number of institutions taking in laundry work from outside and carrying on their trade without the supervision to which all other laundries are subjected. It is common knowledge that owing to the operations of French law the number of these places has largely increased in this country of recent years, and it has been stated that manv thousands of women are now ensured in an unfair competition with those working in laundries to which the existing Factory Act applies. It will be seen from this return that 264 such institutions with laundries are known to exist, and that 204 of these are carried on for the purpose of gain, Of the latter, 117 have expressed their willingness to accept inspection by the Factory Department and 87 declined to accept inspection or gave no definite answer to the Home Office Circular. There were also about 60 others who failed, after a reminder, to reply to it; as to these it is not known, therefore, whether they carried on laundry work for gain or not. 89 As the majority of those known to be trading are willing to be placed under supervision, and as the object of such control aims at nothing more than:— (1) Reasonable hours of work, (2) Healthy conditions of employment, (3) Prevention of accidents from machinery, it seemed only reasonable that the protection of the Act should be made compulsory in all cases, and the Sanitary Committee approved the principle on the recommendation of your Medical Officer of Health. The Bill reached the Second Reading, but was withdrawn on the 6th July. SUMMER FLUSHING OF COURTS. Smoke nuisances were reported at 21 premises during 1905. The number of observations recorded in relation to them amounted to 554, as against 1,202 noted at 55 places in 1904. In all cases the service of the usual notices resulted in the abatement of the nuisance, and in no instance has the evidence been sufficient to justify the institution of legal proceedings for abatement of smoke nuisance. One case is pending, the nuisance at some times being great, but the offenders are reconstructing their furnaces at a considerable cost, and the nuisance will probably not recur. The improvement of the last few years continues, but is only maintained by constant watchfulness of your officers and by the service of intimation Notices where an old nuisance recurs by carelessness or otherwise. In cases where ventilation is properly provided for, the use of electrical radiators for the warming of offices, and gas stoves for cooking will probably in the near future be a partial solution of the London smoke problem. 92 CONFERENCE ON SMOKE ABATEMENT. In September last, the Royal Sanitary Institute issued invitations to all Municipal Authorities throughout the Kingdom to send delegates to a Conference and Exhibition on Smoke Abatement arranged by that body, and in conjunction with the Coal Smoke Abatement Society. The Conference was held at the Horticultural Hall, Westminster, from December 12th to loth, the Chairman of the Sanitary Committee (Mr. G. T. S. Tranter) and your Medical Officer of Health attending as representatives of the Corporation. The question of the abatement of smoke and its attendant evils, including the reduction in daylight and sunshine, has an important bearing on the public health. In addition to the prevention of smoke from factories, much might be done to reduce the volume of smoke from dwellings, which in the aggregate probably amounts to as much as that from factories, by the increased use of gas and electricity and smokeless fuel and by encouraging improved forms of stove and grate construction, and it was in order to ascertain in which way this important problem might best be approached, that the Conference in question was convened. With a view to the collection of accurate information on the subject, a series of questions were submitted to Local Authorities charged by the Public Health Acts with the duty of abating nuisances arising from black smoke. From the 205 replies to these questions, a considerable amount of interesting information may be extracted, and while it is true that a feeling of indifference or hopelessness is manifested by the minority of Sanitary Authorities, the reports show that on the whole when a Local Authority takes steps with the determination to enforce the provisions of the Public Health Acts, it has not experienced insuperable difficulties in minimising the inconvenience and danger to health consequent upon the emission of black smoke. The following subjects were fully discussed during the conference:— Domestic Smoke Abatement. Factory and Trade Smoke Abatement. Administration, Legislation, and necessary reform. As the result of their deliberations, the Conference passed the following Resolution:— "That in view of the need for husbanding our coal resources, the deplorable "and increasing evils produced by smoky fogs, and the desirability of "providing a cheap smokeless fuel for domestic and industrial use, the Councils "of the Royal Sanitary Institute and the Coal Smoke Abatement Society be "asked to join in a memorial to His Majesty's Government setting forth the "urgency of these questions." The Council of the Royal Sanitary Institute decided to record this Resolution and to state that they would join the proposed memorial, subject to its being submitted and approved by them. 93 DAIRIES, COWSHEDS, AND MILKSHOP " ORDERS," 1905. There are now 567 purveyors of milk registered in the City of London under the Dairies, Cowsheds, and Milkshops "Orders" as compared with 552 in 1904. In the course of the year the sale of milk was discontinued at 30 premises, 33 new premises were added to the register, and 36 changes in ownership occurred. The storage of milk has been satisfactory as regards the vessel, utensils and surroundings—the only cases in which interference has been found necessary being three milk vessels unprovided with coverings, and three covers to milk vessels found to be dirty ; in the former cases the covers were at once provided by the proprietors, and in the latter cases the coverings were cleaned in the presence of the Inspector. Two cases of infectious disease have been reported affecting premises occupied by registered purveyors of milk. In one case infectious disease occurred at the residence of a man engaged in a City dairy ; he was suspended from his employment until removal of case to hospital and disinfection of dwelling by Local Authority. In the other case infectious disease occurred on April 19th at Cloth Fair; immediate steps were taken to prevent the sale of milk at the shop until the premises had been disinfected. ICE CREAM. The 85 places in the City where ice creams are made were kept under observation during the season with a view to ensuring that the regulations with regard to its manufacture were properly carried out. Enquiries were also made respecting the conditions under which ice creams were manufactured at the 164 places in other districts supplying the article to tradesmen in the City, and in all cases the replies were satisfactory. There can be no doubt that the legislation with regard to the sale and manufacture of this commodity has been of considerable advantage to the public without placing undue restriction upon the traders. No case of illness attributed to eating ice cream has been reported in the City during the year. The number of itinerant vendors in the City, which had dropped from 47 in 1903 to 19 in 1904, increased to 37 in 1905. 94 There will always be a difficulty in exercising control over trade in ice creams carried out by these itinerant vendors as long as the police have no power of making arrests in those cases where the place of manufacture is not exhibited on the barrow in accordance with the requirements of the law. From enquiries made in outside districts where these men obtained their supplies, it was ascertained that in 20 cases only was the material made under satisfactory conditions. In ten instances the place of manufacture exhibited on the barrow was incorrectly stated, in three cases the name mentioned was unknown at the address given, and in four cases the conditions of manufacture were stated to be unsatisfactory. The premises where ice creams are sold in the City may be classified as under:— Place op Manufacture. Number. In the City. In other districts. Tea shops 115 31 84 Confectioners 19 9 10 Restaurants 12 66 Small confectioners and light refreshment shops 5 4 1 Do. owned by foreigners 98 35 63 Total 249 85 164 From the above it will be seen that of the 103 small confectioners and light refreshment shops in the City where ice creams are sold no less than 98 of them, or 95 per cent., are owned by foreigners. WATERCRESS. In May last the Sanitary Committee referred to me for consideration and review a Report issued by the London County Council on Watercress and Watercress Beds in the neighbourhood of London. On a former occasion I expressed the opinion with regard to this investigation that the Local Government Board were the proper authority for conducting such an enquiry. The Report contains a large number of facts relating to all the known watercress beds "within some fifty miles of the County supplying the "London market," some 129 beds in all, but in no single instance can any of the beds referred to be identified. It is disquieting to find of the samples of water examined there were 22 in which the nitrogenous organic matter was large in quantity, and that in four of them a slight odour of sewage was detected on heating; some of these are reported as being contaminated with sewage matter. There were also 95 seven other samples, all organically of very bad quality, six being reported as undoubtedly polluted with sewage; with one exception these seven samples gave a distinct odour of sewage on heating. The conclusion arrived at by the author of the Report that " it may be assumed that there is under existing circumstances no " material risk in consuming the watercress supplied from the majority "of the beds sending watercress to London," may be justified, but the Report would have fulfilled a more useful purpose if it indicated the situation of the polluted beds. Had the Local Government Board made this investigation the whole of the facts would have been made known, the locality of the beds would have been indicated, and the public would have known those to be avoided as containing elements of danger. This course was adopted by the Local Government Board when reporting upon the sanitary condition of oyster layings round the coast, and has been of the greatest service to those interested in the protection of the public from the danger attending the consumption of polluted food, and they would doubtless have followed this precedent had they been reporting upon watercress beds. 96 MEAT SUPPLIES. Although the weight of meat delivered at the Central Markets during the year 1905 was 4,777 tons in excess of that received in 1904, it failed to equal the deliveries during 1901 or 1903, to which, however, it approximates very closely. It is satisfactory to find that the volume of trade has been so well maintained, especially as the practice of importers of delivering meat ex ship or cold-air stores direct to the retail trade has been more in evidence than in any previous year, in addition to which dep6ts for sale of imported meat to the trade have been opened at Croydon, Kingston, and elsewhere, to which many buyers have been attracted who formerly chose their supplies at Smithfield. The crowded condition of the salesmen's shops and the congested state of the traffic through the Market Avenues point to the conclusion that the maximum amount of business that can be carried on there has been nearly reached unless further accommodation is provided, which would probably be a matter of some difficulty. So greatly does this crowded condition of the Market interfere with the supervision of your officers, that it has become necessary to consider the question of further increasing the staff of Inspectors to cope with it. The returns furnished by the Superintendent of the Market continue to show an increasing ratio of imported to home-grown supplies. In 1896 the proportions were nearly equal, but last year the amount of foreign produce was about three times as large as that of British. This will probably continue so long as the present low prices can be profitably accepted for meat grazed on the Australian downs, the South American pampas, and the Canadian prairies. These advantages are at present driving the home producer altogether out of the race, while the excellent quality of this imported meat shows that the growers oversea can not only undersell the English farmer in his own market, but can also produce an equally good article. Careful breeding for several generations from the best British blood has resulted in " Scotch " cattle reared in Illinois being equal to those found in the markets of Aberdeen or Huntley—"Herefords" bred in Kansas rival those shown at Hereford or Shrewsbury, and American "Shorthorns" are now as good as any to be seen at the great Midland markets. The Canadians are following the example set by their neighbours, and there seems little doubt but that many cattle which the latter send to England are really bred in Canada, and sent to the warmer pastures further south to be fattened. What bearing this may have on the proposal to import Canadian store cattle for fattening in this country it is difficult to say, but it is clear that the 97 Canadians themselves experience no difficulty in making their stores into Beef, as proved by the fact that the fat cattle imported from Canada during the past year have been better than any previously seen from that source. This was foreshadowed in the last Annual Report, and in all probability the movement will continue to develop. Much of the meat which is thus classed as "Foreign" would be more correctly described as "Colonial," but none the less it is seaborne, and our increasing dependence upon it for our food supply cannot fail to emphasise the danger to this country in the event of war with any great naval Power. No doubt many of our home methods of production are obsolete, and it is certainly worth while considering whether the State cannot in some way, whether by State aid or otherwise, assist our farmers in the unequal competition with the great cattle-raising countries. The mutton trade has been fairly well supported lor carcasses of good quality throughout the year, but large over-fat and inferior carcasses have been difficult to sell, and have realised very irregular prices, while the low figure at which New Zealand, Australian, and River Plate sheep can be obtained has rendered the Market unattractive for American and Canadian sheep, which have come in very small numbers, especially towards the close of the year. The importation of chilled meat from the Argentine was first referred to in 1902, and has since increased, until in 1905 it was the predominating feature of the Market. It is unfortunate that the firms engaged in its importation seem to have been desirous of putting as large a quantity as possible on the Market, at any price, rather than by paying more attention to the quality to obtain a more remunerative price. The large quantities on offer, coupled with the fact that it has frequently been out of condition, has had the effect of depressing the value to a very low level, and also to some extent has affected the Market generally. This is doubly unfortunate, as not only does it make this particular business unremunerative, but renders it difficult for other branches of the trade to obtain a reasonable profit. American refrigerated beef has suffered somewhat from Argentine competition, to which, however, it still maintains a marked superiority, although there has been a tendency on the part of some of the importing firms to regard the subject of quality as of secondary importance if only they can compete successfully in price. This is much to be regretted, the tendency should be to improve in quality, as the health of the consuming public is more likely to benefit by a moderate quantity of carefully selected and well preserved meat than by a larger amount of lower quality. The great favour with which refrigerated beef is received is this country led certain persons in New Zealand to attempt sending their productions here 98 in that form, and accordingly 500 quarters of specially selected beef were shipped on the s. s. "Tokomaru," but the length of the journey proved too great, and on its arrival at the Docks it all had to be condemned. About three weeks seems to be the longest period for beef to keep in good condition (the fastest of the Argentine boats invariably land their cargo best), and anything much in excess of that time is sure to bring your Inspectors on the scene. It is, therefore, probably useless for New Zealand to attempt to supply us with refrigerated beef, unless some new and greatly improved machinery can be devised. That the large quantity of refrigerated beef above referred to should have the effect of limiting the demand for a similar article in a frozen state is but a logical conclusion ; hence, as a result, we find that beef from New Zealand and Australia has not for the past year been so much in demand. In that which has been shown on the Market, the requirements of the English trade have been more carefully studied than was formerly the case (more perhaps, than was possible during the period of severe drought in Australia); there also has been a more accurate timing of supply to demand, and a fuller use of that better judgment which is begotten of experience, with the result that there has probably been less of Australian and New Zealand meat condemned than has been the case for several years past. The criticism of its officers' methods and the subsequent representation to the Danish Government at the commencement of the year has had the effect of greatly improving the character of the pork which is sent from there, and although occasionally some is seized as not being up to the required standard, yet a diseased or indifferent carcass is a very rare exception, instead of, as was formerly the case, to be expected, in nearly every consignment. Little or no Russian pork has come, the disturbed state of that country probably precluding the possibility of its export, and the same applies to Servian sheep, which latter are so carelessly selected that salesmen will not be troubled with them. Only one lot has come during the year, of which about two-thirds were seized by your officers. The voluntary inspection of meat exported from the Netherlands continues to show the good results spoken of in last year's Report, and the difference between that which bears the label of the Dutch Government Officials and that which is not labelled is even more marked as time goes on. There is, undoubtedly, a sincere wish on the part of the principal Dutch exporters to comply with our requirements, but they are manifestly placed in an unfair position by the possibility which exists of persons, who for reasons which can only be surmised, sending their produce here without first subjecting it to official examination. With a view to taking steps to equalise matters, Dr. H. Remmelts, Chief of the Government Inspection Service, paid a visit to this country in December, and on several occasions accompanied your Medical Officer and Chief Inspector to the Markets, where demonstration was given of the necessity for compulsory inspection of all meat exported from that country. It is hoped this result may follow his visit. 99 Taking the year as a whole there has been an abundance of meat of indifferent quality, the latter feature being especially marked in the closing months of the year, when so much Argentine beef has come to compete with the trade in carcasses of animals half fattened on the American ranches. Best meat, especially mutton, has for the most part realised satisfactory prices, but it has been weighted down by a superabundance of second and third class qualities, which have not themselves been a good trade. The wharves and cold-air stores have received careful attention, the arrival of shipments in bad condition, especially in one case, having demonstrated its necessity. The following officers have been sent by the Lords Commissioners of the Admiralty for instruction in meat examination, viz., one Superintendent of Victualling Yard, one Deputy Store Officer, one Assistant Inspector of Victualling Stores, six Fleet Paymasters, two Staff Paymasters, four Paymasters and three Assistant Paymasters; all of whom, with one exception, have been granted certificates of competency. The degree of tuberculous disease which is to justify the condemnation of a carcass is still a burning question between meat traders on the one hand and Sanitary Authorities on the other. Whatever may be the case with cattle, there can be no doubt that the necessity for severity in seizure is exercising an influence tending to annihilation of the pig-breeding industry of this country. Probably the practical remedy is an Act of Parliament, of which the principle has already been approved, providing that where it can be shown that the animals were in life apparently sound, the owner should be compensated for their seizure. It is to be hoped that, should the Bill of last Session again be brought forward, it will be found possible to simplify the method of procedure. TUBERCULOUS PORK. The question as to dealing with tuberculous pork has been brought into prominence in the Metropolis during the year owing to Sanitary Authorities, through their medical advisers, acting on the recommendations contained in the Report of the Royal Commission on Tuberculosis, 1898, as ordered by the Local Government Board in circular letters addressed to the Councils of Metropolitan and other Boroughs and of Urban and Rural Districts on the 11th March, 1899, and the 6th September, 1901. With regard to pork, the Commissioners advised— "In view of the greater tendency of generalisation in the pig, we "consider that the presence of tubercular deposit in any degree should "involve seizure of the whole carcass and of the organs." 100 This recommendation has always been carried out in its integrity by your officers, as also generally throughout the Metropolis, and the importance of so doing is emphasized in Dr. G. S. Buchanan's Report to the Local Government Board dated 7th December, 1905, on "Administration in London with regard to meat of pigs affected with Tuberculosis." In considering the relation between human Tuberculosis and Tuberculosis in certain of the lower animals, Dr. Buchanan remarks (page 9) that— "Any person who takes tuberculous matter of animal origin "(containing living tubercle bacilli) into the body along with food, "thereby incurs some risk of acquiring tuberculous disease"* is an inference which is generally drawn and which rests upon a considerable mass of evidence, experimental and clinical. The extent to which meat from tuberculous animals may be responsible for producing human Tuberculosis is, however, a matter in which it is impossible to express an opinion. Relatively to other causes of Tuberculosis it is no doubt small. The Royal Commission of 1898 pointed out that in this country there has been in some quarters a tendency to exaggerate the risk in question; and in commenting on the recommendations of the Commissions before referred to regarding meat of tuberculous pigs, Dr. Buchanan says (pages 9 and. 10):— In Tuberculosis of the bovine animal it is practicable in a great number of instances to distinguish between those internal organs and those parts of the carcass which contain recognisable tubercle bacilli and remaining organs or other parts of the carcass in which there is ground for assumption that no tuberculous matter exists. The working rulesf framed by * Report, Royal Commission on Tuberculosis, 1895, paragraph 22. † "The following principles should be observed in the inspection of tuberculous carcasses of " cattle:— " (a.) When there is miliary Tuberculosis of both lungs "(b.) When tuberculous lesions are present on the "pleura and peritoneum "(c.) When tuberculous lesions are present in the "muscular system, or in the lymphatic glands "embedded in or between the muscles "(d.) When tuberculous lesions exist in any part of an "emaciated carcass The entire carcass and all the organs may be seized. " (a.) When the lesions are confined to the lungs and "the thoracic lymphatic glands " (b.) When the lesions are confined to the liver " (c.) When the lesions are confined to the pharyngeal "lymphatic glands "(d.) When the lesions are confined to any combination "of the foregoing, but are collectively small "in extent The carcass, if otherwise healthy, shal not be condemned, but every part of it containing tuberculous lesions shall be seized. "In view of the greater tendency to generalisation of Tuberculosis in the pig, we consider that the "presence of tubercular deposit in any degree should involve seizure of the whole carcass and of "the organs. "In respect of foreign dead meat, seizure shall ensue in every case where the pleurae have been "'stripped.'" The Local Government Board drew attention to this matter in the circular letters which they addressed to the Councils of Boroughs and Urban and Rural Districts on the 11th March, 1899, and they again referred to it in a circular dated 6t.h September, 1901, and strongly urged Councils to direct those of their officers who were employed as Meat Inspectors to act in accordance with the principles laid down by the Royal Commission. 101 the Royal Commission of 1898 for the guidance of Meat Inspectors were framed with the main object of assisting this distinction, and so obviating needless destruction of meat which may reasonably be regarded as wholesome. Since the inquiries of this Commission and its predecessor of 1895 a considerable number of experiments on lower animals (by feeding and inoculation) have been conducted at Continental slaughterhouses and elsewhere (particularly in Germany) with the object of ascertaining the infective ability under varying conditions of meat from tuberculous animals. I have not had the opportunity of studying the accounts of these experiments at first hand, but so far as I have been able to gather from abstracts which I have seen (e.g., those given in Professor Ostertag's work on "Meat Inspection") their effects as regards bovines is to add to the evidence in favour of making a distinction, on practical and wellrecognised lines, between parts of the carcass which are, and those which are not, to be regarded as likely to contain tubercle bacilli. When, however, the principle of separation of relatively safe from relatively unsafe portions of the carcass comes to be applied to pigs, the difficulty is greater, for the reasons (rapidity of local spread, frequency of generalisation, uncertainty as to the existence of recent generalisation, &c.) already indicated. It was for these substantial reasons that the recommendations of the 1898 Commission to the Meat Inspector in the case of the pig was that "the "presence of tubercular deposit in any degree should involve seizure of "the whole carcass and of the organs." * In Dr. Buchanan's general observations on the inquiry he remarks (page 35) "It would, of course, be another matter if it could be successfully "maintained that there is practically no need for Public Health Authorities "to give themselves any concern as to Tuberculosis in pigs. 1 do not "think, however, that the evidence available (so far as I have been able "to examine it) can be said in any way to warrant the assumption that the "risk to man which arises from eating the meat of pigs which have been "affected by Tuberculosis is negligible, no matter how severely or how "recently these animals have been affected by the disease." With regard to the position of the consumer, Dr. Buchanan says (page 12):— "Before leaving the question of departing from the recommendation "of the 1898 Commission, it is desirable, perhaps, to refer to what * The italics are mine.—M. O. H. 102 "would probably be the attitude towards this proposal of many of the "ordinary consumers of pork—namely, that even if the risk of eating "pork from a tuberculous animal is negligible, they prefer to have their "meat from the animal which is sound, especially when they pay "full price." It would probably be difficult to find anyone who would dissent from this view of the question. As a consequence of the increased numbers of carcasses of tuberculous pigs condemned, there has been great disturbance of the trade. The question is obviously one of considerable importance from the public health point of view, as evidenced by the fact that during the year 1905 your officers condemned in the City no less than 4,631 carcasses of pigs, which accounted for 124 tons of the 168 tons of meat returned as diseased during that year. Much difference of opinion exists on the Continent with regard to the advisability of condemning carcasses of pigs only slightly affected with the disease, but as will be seen in another part of this Report, both the Netherland and Danish Governments have adopted the recommendations of the English Commission with regard to pork exported to this country. Owing to representations made by trade associations, the whole matter has for some time past been under the consideration of the Sanitary Committee, to whom your Medical Officer has presented several Reports on the subject, but Dr. Buchanan's Report to the Local Government Board will probably strengthen their determination to carry out the recommendations of the Royal Commission on Tuberculosis in their entirety. The present, and the only possible position for the Corporation to take, is that laid down by the Report of the Royal Commission on Tuberculosis, and subsequently enjoined by the Local Government Board. As has been constantly pointed out, the inspection at the Market cannot be absolutely complete in detail, inasmuch as in some cases evidence of disease cannot be obtained until the carcass is cut up. It is, therefore, only reasonable that at times pigs with small trace of the disease may escape detection, though the above figures are sufficient proof of the value of the inspection. This consideration, however, will not, it appears, satisfy those who desire perfect protection, which could only be obtained at the cost of dislocation of the whole trade of the Market. 103 IMPORTED CONTINENTAL MEAT. From time to time your Medical Officer of Health has reported on the arrangements made to prevent the distribution in the country of unsound meat, especially pork imported from Holland and Denmark, and in October last the necessity arose for advocating the adoption of similar precautionary measures on the part of the Belgian Authorities. The season for Belgian pigs was just commencing, and already your officers had made two seizures of pigs imported from that country that were markedly affected with Tuberculosis. In each instance the carcasses bore impressions in aniline ink from a rubber stamp, the meaning of which it was impossible to interpret as the characters were blurred and indistinct, but it was subsequently ascertained through the British Vice-Consulate at Ghent that this stamp represented "Maldeghem," a village near Ghent, and was probably impressed at the slaughterhouse on all carcasses leaving the district. Hitherto these pigs came in small numbers, of which few found their way to the City markets, and these only on Saturday. The firms dealing in this trade are chiefly located at Smithfield, but their practice is to meet the boats which bring their pigs and Ostend rabbits at some wharf down the River, and receive their consignments into vans which are taken round to the various provision dealers, who buy what they want from them, so that only unsold articles of both kinds come to Smithfield, unless by special arrangement. Both these pigs and rabbits are sent in closed packages, almost precluding inspection by the officers of the Port Sanitary Authority. It was evident, therefore, that the existing arrangements for preventing the import of unsound meat from Belgium were unsatisfactory, and your Medical Officer of Health advised that the Corporation should ascertain from the Belgian Government if any inspection and marking of meat intended for export is exercised by them, and, if not, whether they would favourably consider the question of adopting a system of control similar to that obtaining both in the Netherlands and in Denmark. This recommendation was adopted, and the matter is now under the consideration of the Belgian Authorities. With regard to the seizure in the City markets of unsound meat that had been inspected and passed as sound for export to this country by the Netherlands and Danish Authorities, the following Tables, prepared from Returns kindly furnished by the respective representatives in this country, show what a small proportion of carcasses from these countries are now found unfit for food. 104 IMPORTED FROM HOLLAND. The attention of the ConsulGeneral for Denmark having been directed to the number of pigs, officially marked as sound, coming into the City market that were found to be affected with Tuberculosis, the following explanation was offered through the Danish Commissioner in a letter addressed to the Town Clerk dated 20th February, 1905:— "When you, in 1903, adopted the recommendations of the Royal "Commission on Tuberculosis, referred to in the Report, these measures, "as previously explained in my letter of 17th November last, were not "generally adopted in Denmark. The unsatisfactory result of this differ"ence in Standards in the two countries has made itself felt, and has been "manifested to the Danish Inspection by the very system of returning "labels. In 1903 I received seven labels, and in 1904 23 labels, from "condemned carcasses of pigs, and of these 23 no less than 16 during "the months of November and December. The Danish Minister of "Agriculture, wishing to remove, as far as possible, every cause for "complaint, has issued, on the 19th of January this year, the following "Order:— "'Supplement to Instruction by the Minister of Agriculture of "'15th August, 1903, to the Veterinary Inspectors appointed to "'control the export of meat. "'As it has been brought to the notice of the Minister that "'carcasses of pigs which have suffered from Tuberculosis, even in a "'very minute degree, are condemned by English Officers of Health "'as unfit for human food, it shall, under reference to Section 4 of "'the Instructions issued on August the loth, 1903, from now "'henceforth be the duty of the Veterinary Inspectors authorised to "'inspect meat for export to retain from export to Great Britain the "'carcass of any pig examined according to the Order of August "'the 15th, 1903, in which, or in the organs of which, is found the "'smallest deposit of a tuberculous nature. "'As the removal of any part of the serous membranes in several "'markets in England causes a suspicion that the animal in question "'has suffered from Tuberculosis, the Veterinary Inspector shall not "'pass for export to Great Britain any carcass of an ox or pig, "'examined according to the said Order, from which any part of "'pleura or peritoneum has been removed. "'January 19th, 1905, "'(Sign) OLE HANSEN, "'Minister of Agriculture. "'H. GRAM.'" 107 "8. Secondly, the Danish Inspection, as explained in a previous com"munication, has hitherto been exclusively sanitary and veterinary. "Carcasses of pigs showing no sign of disease when slaughtered will, "nevertheless, when sent off closely packed before properly cooled, arrive "in a distant market in a state unfit for food. This has been felt more "of late because of a rickety state among many small pigs, making it "unprofitable to feed them off for bacon, has caused them to be killed "young for pork, and in many cases for export. Such carcasses, even "when perfectly sound and fit for food at the place of slaughtering, have "been found unfit for export. Being otherwise sound they have not "been detained by the Inspector. "9. The many seizures of these carcasses, as revealed by the return of "the labels, together with the explanation of your Medical Officer of "Health of the objection to the use of the label on these carcasses, have "caused an extension of the scope of the Danish Inspection by the follow"ing addition to the Instructions issued to the Danish Inspectors of Meat, "which was signed by the Minister of Agriculture on the 18th of this "month:— "'Carcasses of pigs must not be passed for export before they are "'quite cold, and in that state have been found perfectly firm and "'dry. Carcasses of rickety pigs must not be passed for export.'" "The Minister has, at the same time, by an Order of the same date, "taken steps to limit the number of places where pigs can be slaughtered "for export, so as to ensure a better control. "10.
