I am to add that the assistance "thereby to be rendered to the Danish Meat Inspection will be highly "appreciated, and will undoubtedly enable the label of the Danish Govern"ment to be accepted by your Officers as a prima facie guarantee of the "soundness and quality of the meat. "I am, Sir, "Your obedient Servant, "HAROLD FABER." Speaking generally, this communication was most satisfactory, inasmuch as it showed clearly throughout the intention of the Danish Government to deal thoroughly with the question, and, if practicable, to place such a value on their certificate as to practically constitute a guarantee as to the condition of the pork. The Order issued by the Danish Minister of Agriculture on the 19th January, 1905, referred to in the Commission letter, which runs as follows, should be specially noted:— "Supplement to Instructions by the Minister of Agriculture of the "15th August, 1903, to the Veterinary Inspectors appointed to control "the export of Meat. "As it has been brought to the notice of the Minister that carcasses "of pigs which have suffered from Tuberculosis, even in a very minute "degree, are condemned by English Officers of Health as unfit for human "food, it shall, under reference to Section 4 of the Instructions issued "on August the 15th, 1903, from now henceforth be the duty of the "Veterinary Inspectors authorised to inspect meat for export to retain "from export to Great Britain the carcass of any pig examined according "to the Order of August the 15th, 1903, in which, or in the organs "of which, is found the smallest deposit of a tuberculous nature. "As the removal of any part of the serous membranes in several "markets in England causes a suspicion that the animal in question has "suffered from Tuberculosis, the Veterinary Inspector shall not pass for "export to Great Britain any carcass of an ox or a pig examined according "to the said Order from which any part of pleura or peritoneum has "been removed. "January 19th, 1905. "(Sign) OLE HANSEN, "Minister of Agriculture, "H. GRAM." 109 In commenting on this reply, your Medical Officer pointed out that if such instructions were efficiently and properly carried out, any animal passed thereunder and certified would be up to the standard qua disease laid down by the Royal Commission on Tuberculosis; provided that the following special instruction, signed by the Minister of Agriculture on the 18th February, 1905, were also strictly carried into effect:— "Carcasses of pigs must not be passed for export before they are quite "cold, and in that state have, been found perfectly firm and dry. "Carcasses of rickety pigs must not be passed for export." This having been added on account of the many seizures of carcasses, as revealed by the return of the labels, together with the explanation of your Medical Officer of Health. It was further evident that the more frequent condemnations of carcasses from Denmark in 1904 than in 1903 was due to the large increase in the import of Danish pork. Striking evidence is given in the Table on page 105 of the thoroughness with which the Danish Authorities carried out their undertaking to secure a more careful inspection in conformity with the instruction of the Danish Minister for Agriculture. It will be seen from this Table, that, commencing with February, there was a marked decrease in the number of seizures of meat imported from Denmark, when there were only 34 compared with 99 in the previous month, the number dropped to seven in March, one in July, and three only in the last six months of the year. CONTROL OF BACON, &c., INTENDED FOR EXPORT FROM DENMARK. In a Report to the Sanitary Committee of the 28th February, 1905, the question of importation of pork from Denmark and the system of marking adopted in that country was dealt with at some length. On the discussion of the same, the question having arisen as to how far the public is protected from the risk of eating bacon that may have been made in this country from tuberculous "green" bacon imported from Denmark, I made full enquiries herein, when the following facts were ascertained. Since the date of the Report referred to above the Commissioner for the Danish Government has been good enough to send me a translation of the full text of a despatch, referred to in the Report, from the Danish Minister of Agriculture to Veterinary Surgeons authorised to inspect meat for export, which I here reproduce:— Despatch to the Veterinary Surgeons authorised to inspect Meat for Export. The Minister of Agriculture has this day given the order that fresh carcasses of pigs, parts of such and offal of pigs shall not be passed for export to Great Britain if they are of rickety pigs. 110 Fresh carcasses of pigs shall not, in any case, be passed for export before they are quite cooled, and after cooling have been found perfectly firm and dry. In accordance with supplement of the 19th of January this year to Instruction of the 15th of August, 1903, fresh carcasses of pigs, parts of such and offal shall not be passed for export to Great Britain, when they are produced from pigs in which or in the organs of which is found the smallest deposit of a tuberculous nature. Neither shall be passed for export to Great Britain carcasses of pigs and oxen from which any part of the membranes of the chest or abdomen has been removed. The Veterinary Surgeons authorised to inspect meat for export will carefully note these instructions and act accordingly. The 18th of February, 1905. OLE HANSEN, Minister of Agriculture. The following is a translation of the Danish law of the 24th April, 1903, on the control of the export of fresh and lightly salted meat, &c.:— Section 1.—The Government shall have power to take the necessary steps to prevent the exportation from the country of fresh and lightly salted meat as well as offal of horses, cattle, sheep, goats, and swine, when it has become putrid or is derived from animals suffering from disease, making it unfit for human food, or from animals emaciated so as to make their flesh unfit for food. Section 2.—Meat and offal which shall be found totally unfit for human food by the Inspectors appointed under this Law to control the export shall under necessary precautions be rendered useless for such purpose: according to regulations to be issued by the Minister of Agriculture. Section 3.—It shall not be lawful for any person in this country to offer for sale or to sell as human food meat and offal of dead horses, cattle, sheep, goats, and swine, fresh or in any way prepared. Such meat and offal shall not be exported from this country in any form whatsoever which would allow it to be used as human food. Section 4.—It shall not be lawful for any person in this country to offer for sale or to sell as human food meat and offal of horses, cattle, sheep, goats, and swine which before slaughtering are found to be diseased, or which after slaughtering show signs of marked diseased conditions, nor shall such meat and offal be exported from this country in any form whatever which would allow it to be used as human food, unless a registered Veterinary Surgeon shall certify that the meat and offal is fit for human food, according to regulations to be issued by the Minister of Agriculture. 111 Section 5.—Every person guilty of an offence against this Law or against any regulation issued under this Law shall, unless the offence shall make him liable to more severe punishment, be liable to a fine of no less than 20 Kr. (£l) and not exceeding 1,000 Kr. (£55), to be paid to the Treasury of the State, while the goods in relation to which the offence has been committed shall be forfeited. The forfeited goods shall be rendered useless for human food by action of the Police. The offender shall, in case of a second or subsequent conviction, be liable to a double fine, and under aggravating circumstances to imprisonment, in which case the conviction shall be published by action of the Police. Prosecutions under this Law shall be brought before the public Police Courts. Section 6.—This Law shall replace the Law of 22nd March, 1897, on the control of the export of fresh meat, and shall come into operation on the 1st September, 1903. Christian R. OLE HANSEN. Under the provisions of this Law a Ministerial Decree was issued on the 15th August, 1903, which was revised on the 18th February, 1905. The following is a translation of the latter:— Translation of Order concerning the Export of Meat and Offal of Horses, Cattle, Sheep, Goats, and Swine, 18th February, 1905. The Minister of Agriculture, by virtue of the power given him by Law of 24th April, 1903, on the control of the export of meat, &c., does hereby, concerning the export of fresh and slightly salted meat (and offal) of horses, cattle, sheep, goats and swine for future observance, order as follows:— Section 1.—Fresh and lightly salted meat and offal (including viscera) of horses, cattle, sheep, goats and swine shall not be exported from this country unless accompanied by a certificate from a Veterinary Surgeon appointed for that purpose, stating that the meat, &c., is not putrid and not derived from animals suffering from any disease, making the meat, &c., unfit for human food, or from animals so emaciated as to make their flesh unfit for human food, according to the following regulations. Section 2.—To each piece of fresh meat weighing 8 pounds or more shall be attached, by means of a wire or string, a label (see Section 5) describing the label, mode of fixing it, and the certificate to be issued by 112 the veterinary inspector and the sender, in the words of previous Order of 15th August, 1903). Section 3.—Fresh meat in pieces weighing less than 8 pounds, lightly salted meat, including bacon, fresh and lightly salted offal (including viscera) of horses, cattle, sheep, goats and swine, shall not be exported unless the packing (barrel, case, wrapper, &c.) shall bear a label to be issued by the Minister (see Section 5), and shall be tied round with a rope or wire sealed by the Veterinary Inspector in such a way that it shall be impossible to remove the contents without breaking the seal. To bacon shall be used the same labels which, by Section 2, have been ordered to be used for fresh meat. They shall be fixed by means of wire or rope on the outside of the bale so as to be visible to the Inspectors controlling the export, and sealed with a lead seal so as to make it impossible to remove the contents without breaking the seal. With each consignment shall be sent a certificate signed manu propria by the Inspector and the consignor as follows:— "This is to certify that I have examined the meat (or offal) "packed in (number of) barrels (or cases, baskets or bales) "destined for export, and that I have found it fit for export according "to Law of 24th April, 1903, on control of export of meat, &c.; "further, that I have this day attached to the packing the prescribed "export labels No " (Address) (Date) (Name) "Veterinary Surgeon authorised under Law "of 24th April, 1903." "This certificate shall be valid for the controlling authorities on the "place of exportation for five days only. "As consignor of (number of) barrels (cases, baskets or bales) "containing meat (or offal), and marked... , I hereby solemnly "declare that these barrels (&c.) contain only the meat (offal) "examined by the Veterinary Inspector, as mentioned in the "certificate above. (Address) (Date) (Name) Section 4. —It shall not be lawful for the Veterinary Inspector to sign the certificates mentioned in Sections 2 and 3 unless he has inspected the animals during or after slaughtering, and, if after 113 slaughtering, then before the carcass has been cut through and while all the viscera are still present so as to be identified. The Veterinary Inspector shall not sign the certificate mentioned in Section 3 unless he has either been present while the meat (offal) was packed in the barrel (case, bale, &c.), or has taken precautions against possible infringements by seeing that all meat or offal present in the slaughtery and unfit for human food has been destroyed (by boiling, cutting to pieces, by petroleum or otherwise). With regard to bacon, he shall not sign the certificate unless he has been present at the wrapping of the bales or in other reliable way has obtained security that the bales contain only such bacon which has been passed as fit for export. The Veterinary Inspector shall not sign the certificates mentioned in Sections 2 and 3 unless the meat has been found absolutely fit for human food according to the instructions issued by the Minister of Agriculture to the Inspectors. Section 5.—The Minister of Agriculture will make arrangements for the supply of the labels, lead seals, squeezers and printed schedules mentioned in Sections 2 and 3. Section 6.—Every person intending to export meat abroad shall send to the Chief Constable of Police a notice in writing applying for permission to do so. Such notice shall, when dealing with an alreadyexisting business, state how many (1) horses, (2) cattle, (3) sheep, (4) goats, and (5) swine have been slaughtered during the last year, and shall, by the Chief Constable, be forwarded to the Minister of Agriculture, who will inform the Chief Constable how far the application can be granted. The Chief Constable shall at the same time communicate to the Minister which Veterinary Surgeon he proposes the Minister to authorise to carry on the control, and how it is proposed to fix the remuneration for the control. The question of remuneration, which shall be defrayed by the sender, shall be finally decided by the Minister of Agriculture. The Minister can at any time withdraw the authorisation as regards both the slaughtery and the Veterinary Inspector. If necessary for the control, several Veterinary Inspectors can be appointed within the same Police District, in which case the Chief Constable shall decide which slaughteries each Inspector shall control, and inform the Minister thereon. The Veterinary Inspector shall have access to the slaughtery at any time. The same power shall be vested in such Inspectors which the Minister of Agriculture might send. 114 The Veterinary Inspector shall follow the instructions given him by the Minister of Agriculture. He shall enter in an authorised journal, sent him by the Minister, all details concerning the control of the export. He shall produce this journal for the Inspector which the Minister might send. If the Veterinary Inspector should have reason to suspect that the Law of 24th April, 1903, or any regulation under this Law, are being infringed, he shall at once inform the Chief Constable. Section 7.—In case of exportation by ship it shall be the duty of the Customs, and in case of exportation by railway it shall be the duty of the stationmaster, to ascertain whether the regulations in Sections 1, 2 and 3 are carried out, and that the goods are not exported to another country than the one stated on the certificates. Section 8.—Consignments of meat destined for abroad, which by their smell prove to be putrid, shall, even if accompanied by the prescribed certificates and otherwise fulfilling the regulations, be detained at the place of export. Notice of such detention shall at once be given to the Police for further action. Section 9.—The preceding regulations do not apply in the case of meat and offal supplied for ship's stores, nor in the case of meat in small pieces exported over the frontier for household use. Section 10.—Meat and offal submitted to the Inspector for export and found to be unfit for human food according to the instructions issued by the Minister of Agriculture shall, under the direction of the Inspector and at the cost of the owner, be made useless for food by heating, boiling, destroying in special ovens, by petroleum or by burying with lime. This Order shall replace the Order by the Minister of Agriculture, Number 142, of loth of August, 1903, and shall come into operation on the 15th of March, 1905. OLE HANSEN. 18th February, 1905. H. Gram. Since the 15th March, 1905, therefore, Danish "green "Bacon has been subject to the same Regulations that are in force in that country with regard to fresh meat, and labels similar to those used for the latter are affixed to every bale exported to this country from Denmark. Hitherto Danish Bacon factories have been regulated partly by the various local Public Health Authorities. 115 The following is a statement of the quantity of Bacon imported from Denmark during the seven years 1899-1905, and has considerable interest as bearing on the subject:— Year. Value. To United Kingdom. To London. £ Tons. Tom. 1899 2,945,757 60,530 52,193 1900 3,058,782 54,731 48,707 1901 3,234,456 53,045 45,552 1902 3,749,108 62,781 52,721 1903 4,294,017 74,805 60,051 1904 4,532,420 86,194 70,315 1905 3,978,317 73,584 61,423 From November, 1899, to October, 1900, the bacon was shipped to Thameshaven. From November, 1900, to August, 1901, it was landed at Cotton's Wharf, Tooley Street. Since the latter date it has come to London viâ Harwich and the Great Eastern Railway. The Danish Commissioner kindly furnished these figures, which show that during the seven years referred to, nearly 84 per cent. of the bacon imported into the United Kingdom from Denmark was consigned to London. The Danish Authorities, both in this country and Denmark, have shown every desire that their meat exports should have a good reputation in the world's markets, and have recently accepted the principles of the Royal Commission with regard to tuberculous meat. These precautions should be amply sufficient to protect the public, and provided they are carried out in their entirety, there is no reason to fear that bacon made from pork imported from Denmark would be more likely to be tubercular than that prepared from animals killed in the United Kingdom. Two cases of seizure of tuberculous pigs' heads consigned to City markets by a firm of bacon curers in the country occurred during the year, but having regard to all the facts no prosecution was instituted. In view of the fact that tubercle bacillus is not destroyed in the process of manufacture, and further, that in the town where the factory is situated there is no inspector, according to the statement of the company, it was thought advisable to bring the matter before the notice of the Local Government Board and the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries. 116 Table showing the annual deliveries and other details during the past 32 years:— MEAT SUPPLIES, 1874 TO 1905. It is satisfactory to note that the decrease in the number of cases of milk adulteration is maintained, as shown by the following figures:— Year 1901. 1902. 1903. 1904. 1905. Number of Milk samples examined 392 599 517 452 481 Found genuine 209 519 457 417 445 Found adulterated 83 80 60 35 36 Percentage of adulteration 21.0 13.3 11.6 7.5 7.5 127 Milk being an article that can be so readily sophisticated, fraudulent milk dealers will always exist, and only a rigorous enforcement of the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts can keep the evil within bounds. The above Table shows very clearly what can be done in this direction, and if only the offenders were more heavily punished for their misdeeds, it cannot be doubted that the quality of the milk sold in the City would be still further improved. Of three samples purchased at shops in the early morning on Sunday, one was impoverished by the abstraction of 4 per cent. of fat, and two out of five samples taken on Sunday evening were adulterated by the addition of water to the extent of 4 per cent. and 8 per cent. respectively. Thirty-three samples were taken from perambulating milk sellers both during the day and in the early morning. Of these, three were adulterated, and prosecutions ensued in each case. Two of the defendants were fined, and in one case the summons was dismissed on proof of warranty. Fifteen samples were taken at the door in course of delivery, and of these four were adulterated. Three of the sellers were prosecuted, and in each instance a warranty defence was successful. The fourth case was not proceeded with, as the Magistrate had taken it into consideration in imposing a fine of £10 and costs in another one previously heard. As before noted, milk adulteration in the City has been reduced from 21 per cent. to 7.5 per cent. after four years' active administration of the Acts; and in this respect the City compares favourably with the rest of the Metropolis, as shown by the following Table compiled from the last Report of the Local Government Board:— Percentage of samples of Milk adulterated. in the City of London and the several Metropolitan Boroughs during 1904, arranged in "order of merit." Hampstead 173 2.3 Lewisham 258 10.9 St. Marylebone 260 2.7 Lambeth 515 11.1 Holborn 256 5.1 Stoke Newington 61 11.5 Deptford 215 6.0 Poplar 130 11.6 Kensington 213 6.6 Southwark 1,183 12.8 Hammersmith 130 6.9 Finsbury 346 13.8 Bermondsey 469 7.0 Bethnal Green 378 14.5 Camberwell 318 7.5 Wandsworth 400 15.5 City of London 469 7.5 Fulham 245 16.8 Greenwich 118 7.6 Battersea 452 17.0 Islington 601 7.8 Stepney 641 19.7 Woolwich 370 9.2 Shoreditch 365 23.6 Chelsea 178 9.5 St. Pancras 380 25.3 Paddington 289 10.0 Hackney 425 25.9 Westminster 772 10.4 128 A reference to p. 140 will show that 23 prosecutions were instituted against milk sellers during the year, in which convictions were obtained in 13 instances, and in no less than ten cases warranty defences were successful. With regard to this form of defence, there can be no question that the opportunities afforded by the warranty clauses of the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts are exceedingly prejudicial to the effective administration of the law. This question was the subject of a Special Report presented by your Medical Officer to the Sanitary Committee last January, wherein an amendment of the existing law was advocated. The Board of Agriculture and Fisheries have also had their attention directed to this matter, and in their Annual Report for last year referred to two suggestions for dealing with the difficulty. "The one is that whenever a warranty is pleaded, the Local Authority can "have the summons adjourned in order to apply for a summons, or in the "case of successive warranties, for summonses against the warrantor or "warrantees, and the magistrate shall then hear the two or more summonses "as one case, so as to decide which is the guilty party. The other is that the "warranty sections should be repealed, and the seller of the milk be left to his "remedy at common law against the person from whom he purchased it." The latter appears to be the best remedy by reason of its simplicity, and the Board of Agriculture and Fisheries are also of opinion that it is the one that would commend itself to the agricultural community. Stoke Newington 21 None St. Pancras 183 7.6 Westminster 211 0.9 Wandsworth 180 7.8 Kensington 1ll 1.8 Paddington 67 8.9 Hampstead 110 1.9 Islington 252 9.1 St. Marylebone 75 2.6 Hackney 196 9.2 City of London 441 2.9 Bethnal Green 104 9.6 Southwark 355 4.8 Bermondsey 179 10.6 Chelsea 60 5.0 Finsbury 120 10.9 Woolwich 78 5.1 Shoreditch 181 11.0 Greenwich 91 5.5 Camberwell 301 13.6 Lambeth 250 5.6 Battersea 114 14.4 Deptford 97 6.2 Fulham 369 15.4 Holborn 73 6.8 Stepney 237 15.6 Lewisham 166 7.2 Hammersmith 76 17.1 Poplar 121 7.4 A case of some interest in relation to the character of some of the butter imported from Holland occurred during the year. A provision merchant at Smithfield received a consignment of butter from Rotterdam, and having reason to doubt its genuineness, requested that a sample might be taken. This was done, with the result that it was certified by the Public Analyst to contain 20 per cent. of margarine (fat and other than butter fat). This discovery placed the salesman in the following position:— 1. He was liable to a penalty of £20 for importing margarine containing more than ten per cent. of butter fat. 2. It obviously could not be sold as butter. 3. It would be unlawful to sell it as margarine. 4. The consignors in Holland threatened to denounce him to their fellow-dealers because he declined to pay for the mixture, 5. As the consignors had guaranteed its purity, the salesman would, if so requested, have sold it with a warranty, in which case he would have been liable to a conviction. This would have placed his tenancy in jeopardy, as in due course his conviction would have been reported to the Markets Committee. This matter was subsequently put into the hands of the Butter Association, and resulted in the Netherland Authorities undertaking to take legal 130 proceedings in Holland against the consignors, provided the English importers undertook to defray the cost of prosecution, it being a penal offence in Holland not to label as margarine any such article intended for export. SALE OF BUTTER BILL, 1905. This Bill was introduced by the Government in the public interest on the 28th March, 1905. Since the 22nd April, 1902, a Regulation has been in operation with regard to water in Butter, made by the Board of Agriculture under the powers conferred upon them by section 4 of the Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1899. The Regulation is to the following effect:— "When the proportion of water in a sample of Butter exceeds 16 per "cent. it shall be presumed for the purposes of the Sale of Food and "Drugs Acts, 1875 to 1899, until the contrary is proved, that the Butter "is not genuine, by reason of the excessive amount of water therein." Section 1 of the Bill definitely limits the permissible amount of water in Butter to sixteen per cent. without any reservation. Moreover, any person who manufactures, sells or exposes for sale any Butter to which any substance has been added whereby the amount of water has been increased will be rendered liable to penalties of £20, £50 and £100 for the first, second and third offence respectively. This measure was introduced by the Government with the object of protecting the public from a form of sophistication which the law at present is powerless to prevent. Certain traders are now, and had for some time past, been selling an article containing as much as 25 per cent. of water, which has been ingeniously incorporated with genuine Butter and subsequently sold under the guise of "Milk-blended Butter." It is true that the makers of this article protect themselves from prosecution under the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts by disclosing the fact to the purchaser that the article is a mixture, but the ordinary buyer can scarcely be expected to know that the milk so added itself contains from 86 to 88 per cent. of water as one of its natural constituents. The buyer is deceived, because, although "milk-blended" Butter is sold at a lower price than genuine Butter, being loaded with water, it is in fact dearer, and he is consequently ignorant of the real composition of the material he is buying. The main object of this Bill was to stamp out this unfair trading, although such measure is also desirable upon other grounds. From the health point of view, milk-blended Butter is objectionable, as such admixture seriously impairs its nutritive properties, the excess of water being obviously without nourishing properties, and consequently lessens its food value. Its use, therefore, might seriously impair the health of persons placed by force of circumstances or medical advice upon a low diet. In addition to 131 these objections, its keeping properties are also lessened by the addition of water, especially daring warm weather, unless heavily charged with preservatives. It is further stated that the sale of this stuff has opened the door to another questionable sophistication. It was formerly made from Australian Butter; now, however, imported Siberian Butter is used, which can be obtained at a cheaper rate; this is amalgamated with a mixture of skimmed milk and cocoanut oil, and is placed upon the market as a "Milk-blended" Butter. The Bill will prevent this fraud, but if Irish firkin butter containing 20 per cent. of water is legalised for a time as proposed in the Bill, it may lead to the practice of a form of deception that would greatly prejudice the Irish farmers the Bill is intended to protect. In this connection a firm of produce merchants in the North of England have recently made a very interesting exposure of the methods adopted by unscrupulous butter manufacturers, and it may not be without interest to the public to learn how the Siberian product can be converted into mock Irish Firkin Butter. The following details are given:—* MOCK IRISH FIRKIN BUTTER. If the manipulator buys his Siberian Butter at the right time, that is to say in the summer months, he can produce Mock Irish Firkin Butter in the winter at summer prices. The cost of Siberian Butter is estimated on this basis—plus the expenses of cold storage. MODE OF OPERATION. The treated milk is taken cold and placed in a chamber where peat is burning, such as is used by provision merchants to smoke their bacon. It is only allowed to remain here sufficiently long to impart a slightly peaty and smoky flavour, such as one often finds in Irish butter. Note.— The milk must be much colder than the chamber or it will not absorb. The amalgamation is then carried out as follows: Equal weights of the solidified milk and butter are kneaded well together in a kneading machine, after this the remaining quantity of butter is added, and then, whilst the machine is going, the treated milk, previously and quickly heated to a temperature of about 37° cent. or 100° Fahr., is slowly added. The higher the temperature of the butter when going into the machine, the more glossy or vaseline-like will be the product. Should more salt be required, this may be added; but the salt from the preparation of the solidified milk is usually sufficient Several days are required before the butter again assumes its texture. Then it should be packed into firkins. The cost of these are from 2s. 6d. to 3s. per cwt. Farmer's Lump Butter is at the present time being treated in a somewhat similar manner, viz.:—loaded with extra water and solids. * From "Butter Manipulation." Showing "How the British Public are cheated, and many of His Majesty's "Colonial subjects robbed of their just due."By George Wall and Co., Liverpool. Printed 1905. 132 PROCESSES AND PROFITS. Table showing Contents when Siberian Butter is converted into Mock Irish Firkin Butter. 81½ lbs. Butter at 88s. per cwt. = £3 4s. 0d. Allowing for deduction of curds and moisture present, reduces the butter fat weight to 74 lbs., the cost of which remains the same. £ s. d. 74 lbs. Butter Fat 3 4 0 19 lbs. Treated Milk 0 0 2¼ 7 lbs. Solidified Milk 007 100 lbs. £3 4 9¼ Or equal to £3 12s. 6d. per cwt. The following shows the rate of gain obtained by the process, and the relative prices per cwt. and per lb.:— 112 lbs. Original Butter costs 88s. = £4 8s. 0d.; or about 9½d. per lb. 112 lbs. after Manipulation costs 72s. 6d. = 3 12s. 6d.; or about 7¾d. per lb. Gain £0 15s. 6d.; or nearly 1¾d. per lb. This mixture contains nothing which is foreign (analytically) to Irish butter as now sold and passed by the Public Analyst, who would certify it about as follows:— Butter Fats 74.0 Curd, Salt, &c. 8.0 Moisture 18.0 (No Foreign Fat) 100.0 The "Treated Milk" and "Solidified Milk" referred to in the foregoing is thus prepared:— SOLIDIFIED MILK.— How to Prepare. Take some 1,000 lbs. sweet separated milk, which can be obtained at a cost of 1d. per gallon of 10¼ lbs. Heat up to 32° Cent., gently stir, and pour in 200 grammes hydrochloric acid which has been previously diluted with ten times its own weight of water. The action of this acid will separate the casein from the whey in the course of a few minutes. When completed pour off the liquid, drain and press the casein until its weight is reduced to one-tenth the original weight of the milk. This should then be placed in a kneading machine, with enough carbonate of soda to neutralise all acid present. (N.B.— This product must be quite neutral to blue and red litmus test.) The action of the soda converts the casein into a jelly-like substance. This preparation, when found by the analyst in butter, is indicated as curd and salt, both of which are usually present in certain small proportions in best pure butter ; the action of the hydrochloric acid and the carbonate of soda resulting in the formation of common salt, tho chemical equation of which is about as follows:— Na2C03 + 2HCl=2NaCl + C02 + H20 The cost of milk thus solidified is about one penny per lb. 133 TREATED MILK.— How to Make. Separated skim milk, as in the previous instance, is taken, and after being pasteurised is again heated to a temperature of about 80° Fah. It is then infected with a pure culture of lactic ferment, the same that imparts to butter most of its flavour. The following is the chemical equation of the change supposed to take place:— C12H22 O11+OH2=4O3 H6 O3 The acid is allowed to develop to a known quantity, as ascertained by a further delicate test, which it is not necessary to here describe. This point having been reached the milk is now rapidly cooled, and will then be found to have a slightly acid taste, not unlike buttermilk just after churning. Milk so treated and added to butter produces a very delicate flavour and butter aroma. The cost is about one penny per gallon, or one-tenth of a penny per lb.* The Bill shared the fate of somewhat similar Butter Bills introduced on previous occasions, and of this one there is recorded: "Order for Second Reading read and discharged. Bill withdrawn, 20th July, 1905." COFFEE. It is to the discredit of dealers that there is far too much coffee adulteration in the City. The former was a sample of sophisticated Dutch butter, referred to in my notes on Butter on p. 129, but is not included in the Table on p. 128; the "four ales" were taken under the following circumstances:— 135 ARSENIC IN BEER. In November last, notification was received from a medical man that an illness had come under his treatment, the symptoms of which suggested arsenical poison. The patient had been drinking "more than usual," and had favoured a particular kind of beer known as "Four Ale," obtained from certain public-houses in his district. The diagnosis of arsenical poisoning was strengthened by the fact that traces of this material were found in his hair. Samples of the ale in question were purchased at the public-houses implicated and submitted to the Public Analyst, who reported that they contained a trace of Arsenic less than 1/100 grain per gallon. The Royal Commission in their Report* definitely stated that it was not desirable to fix any standard of Arsenic permissible in beers, as "allowance has to be made for the possibility of other small quantities "of Arsenic being received at the same time from more than one source "of food and drink, and suspectible people have to be considered." It is, however, generally understood that while it may be possible to produce beers absolutely "Arsenic-free," that the majority, when brewed mainly from malt and hops, will contain minute traces, which will, however, be below the amount likely to produce any harmful effects. In two cases the brewers of the samples were identified; in the third case they could only be assumed, as the public-house was "free," and obtained its supplies from various sources. The several makers were at once communicated with, and were found to be fully alive to the importance of taking the utmost precautions. All the details of their processes of manufacture, and the systems of safeguarding the public by the constant examination of all materials as well as the finished article, were explained by the chemical experts advising the brewer in each case. From careful consideration it would seem that no steps are omitted to render the beer practically "Arsenic-free," and that, unless possible in the case of very susceptible persons, no real danger can arise. Further samples will be taken from time to time in order to check the value of the above system. ALIENS AND THE SALE OF FOOD AND DRUGS ACTS. Attention should be called to the fact that, under certain circumstances, aliens who are convicted of offences for which imprisonment can be inflicted without the option of a fine, are liable to be deported under the Aliens Act. The following are the provisions of the above Acts showing what offences under these statutes render those committing them liable to imprisonment without the option of a fine:— Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1875, Sections 3 and 4. "3.— No person shall mix, colour, stain or powder, or order, "or permit any other person to mix, colour, stain or powder, any article * Final Report of the Royal Commission appointed to enquire into arsenical poisoning from the consumption of beer and other articles of food or drink, dated 6th November, 1903. 136 "of food with any ingredient or material so as to render the article "injurious to health, with intent that the same may be sold in that state, uand no person shall sell any such article so mixed, coloured, stained or "powdered, under a penalty in each case not exceeding fifty pounds for the "first offence; every offence, after a conviction for a first offence, shall "be a misdemeanour, for which the person, on conviction, shall be "imprisoned for a period not exceeding six months with hard labour. "4.— No person shall, except for the purpose of compounding "as hereinafter described, mix, colour, stain or powder, or order, or "permit any other person to mix, colour, stain or powder, any drug "with any ingredient or material so as to affect injuriously the quality "or potency of such drug, with intent that the same may be sold in that "state, and no person shall sell any such drug so mixed, coloured, stained "or powdered, under the same penalty in each case respectively, as in "the preceding section for a first and subsequent offence." Sale of Food and Drugs Act, 1891. "17.— (1) Where under any provision of the Sale of Food "and Drugs Act, 1875, a person guilty of an offence is liable to a fine "which may extend to twenty pounds as a maximum, he shall be liable "for a second offence under the same provision to a fine not exceeding "fifty pounds, and for any subsequent offence to a fine not exceeding one "hundred pounds." This covers all the offences under the Act of 1875, except those created by section 3, as to mixing, &c., any article of food with any ingredients, so as to render the same injurious to health, and selling same; by section 4, as to mixing, &c., drugs with ingredients so as to affect injuriously the quality, dec., of the drug; by section 17, for refusing to sell to any officer; and by section 27, for forging a certificate of warranty. "(2) Where, under any provision of the Sale of Food and Drugs "Acts, a person guilty of an offence is liable to a fine exceeding fifty "pounds, and the offence in the opinion of the Court was committed by "the personal act, default, or culpable negligence of the person accused, "that person shall be liable (if the Court is of opinion that a fine will "not meet the circumstances of the case), to imprisonment, with or with"out hard labour, for a period not exceeding three months." This includes the offences covered by sub-section 1, those created by the Margarine Act, and those created by this Act under sections 1, 7, 8, 16, but only the third offence under any of these provisions. It is important to notice that the Court has no power to imprison under this sub-section, unless in the opinion of the Court the offence, that is, the third offence under the above-mentioned provisions, was committed by the personal act, default or culpable negligence of the person accused. There is a direct connection between the Sale of Food and Drug's Act and the Aliens Act that came into operation at the beginning of this year, and in 137 the administration of the former, section 3 (1) (a) of the latter should be . borne in mind in dealing with those cases where the defendant is an alien who has committed an offence under either of the above-quoted sections of the Sale of Food and Drugs Acts, 1875-1891, that render him liable to imprisonment without the option of a fine. The following is the text of the Aliens Act referred to: — Aliens Act. "Section 3 (1).— The Secretary of State may, if he thinks fit, make an "order (in this Act referred to as an expulsion order) requiring an "alien to leave the United Kingdom within a time fixed by the order, "and thereafter to remain out of the United Kingdom. "(a) If it is certified to him by any court (including a court "of summary jurisdiction), that the alien has been convicted by "the court of any felony or misdemeanour or other offence for "which the court has power to impose imprisonment without the " option of a fine and that the court recommend that the expulsion should be made in his case either in addition to or in "lieu of his sentence." DIVISION OF SAMPLES CONTAINED IN PACKETS. Smith v. Savage. In this case, heard before the Lord Chief Justice, in the King's Bench Division, on the 12th April, 1905, an inspector had bought four packets of cream of tartar from one box. He emptied the four into one place and mixed them up and divided the mixture into three parts. The Justices dismissed the summons and the case was taken to a higher Court, which allowed the appeal upon the following grounds. The case was different from that of Mason v. Cowdary upon which the respondent relied when the case was before the Magistrates. Some question might arise if different articles not of a similar name or articles coming from different people were mixed together in the same shop; that, however, was not the case in this instance. Here the appellant has asked for cream of tartar, which, for the purpose of measurement, was put up in penny packets. He said he would take four packets, and they had to determine whether, because four packets of the same article, labelled in the same way and sold at the same time as cream of tartar, were mixed together and then divided into three parts, it was a good objection to the analysis that followed. The Court was of opinion that it was not a good objection, and sent the case back to the Justices for trial. 138 139 SALE 0F F00D AND DRUGS ACTS, 1875-1899. SUMMARY. £ s. d. £ s. d. First Quarter: Fines 44 1 0 Expenses 9 3 6 Second Quarter „ 5 15 0 ,, 4 7 6 Third Quarter „ 28 3 6 ,, 5 14 6 Fourth Quarter „ 73 0 0 ,, 5 5 0 £150 19 6 £24 10 6 Twenty-six fines were inflicted during the year, as against 57, 23 and 27 in the preceding three years. The average fines and expenses imposed was £6 15s., the corresponding amounts for 1902, 1903 and 1904 being £4 2s. 2d., £4 18s. 1d., and £2 19s. 2½d. respectively. By direction of the Sanitary Committee eighteen persons were cautioned during the year as to the questionable or unsatisfactory character of articles they sold. I have the honour to be, Gentlemen, Your obedient Servant, W.Muyredfe Public Health Department, Medical Officer of Health for the Guildhall, E.C., City of London. March, 1906. APPENDICES. Lyons and Co., Ltd. 166 Appendix E. KITCHENS OF RESTAURANTS. Extract from Annual Report of Medical Officer of Health for 1904. The experience gathered from the constant inspection of these places suggests the following:— The floor, walls and ceiling should be finished in a durable material and be free from obstructions, so as to be easily cleansed, all tables, benches, &c., being readily movable. Where gas is used for cooking purposes, means should be provided for carrying off the fumes of gas Electric light should be used, so as to reduce heat as far as possible in the kitchen. Proper larders and pantries should be provided for the storage of cooked and uncooked food. Special attention should be paid to the condition of water cisterns, and effectual means taken to exclude dust and dirt, &c. At least one white enamelled stoneware sink should be provided for washing vegetables; indeed, it would be better if all sinks were made of similar material, the advantage being that dirty conditions are at once apparent and call for immediate attention. Cupboards or other provision outside the kitchen should be made for hanging wearing apparel when not in use. Means should he taken to exclude dust and dirt from entering underground kitchens as far as possible. Where inspection chambers to drains exist in kitchens they should be provided with double covers (one cover below another), or be so arranged that the covers cannot become unsealed by being walked on, &c. Separate lavatory basin accommodation should be provided outside the kitchen for both sexes, where practicable, for use of employés. Where lift shafts are used in connection with kitchens they should be cleansed from time to time. 167 In a properly arranged kitchen many other things suggest themselves, such as separate scullery and cold (ice) larder, separate rooms for cooked and uncooked foods, fish larder, vegetable store, dry goods store, plate room, saucepan room, &c., &c. It is almost unnecessary to add that kitchens should be adequately ventilated and that the temperature should not exceed 80° Fahr. Many persons appear to imagine the cooking can only be carried on in an extremely hot kitchen, but this is a wrong impression. The lack of proper provision for keeping food hot whilst being served up often results in the temperature being kept unduly high, the windows being closed to prevent the rapid cooling of the dishes. It is found that the businesses are constantly changing hands, and that the kitchen arrangements are an ever-varying quantity. Gas stoves are frequently added, without any provision being made for carrying off the fumes, the water-closet accommodation for the sexes has to be re-adjusted and the walls and ceilings of the kitchens soon get dirty. These and many other points need constant and vigilant attention to ensure a decent standard being maintained. Special attention is now being paid to the storage of food, and suggestions from my Department are often appreciated and acted upon.
